FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
BlackRock and Google in talks over joint venture; Technology

By Aime Williams 
594 words
19 October 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
10
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Fund house and tech giant aim to pursue artificial intelligence

BlackRock is in discussions with Google, the technology group, to create a joint venture that the world's largest fund house hopes will enable it to improve returns.

The tie-up, which would be a first for the fund market, will seek to explore how artificial intelligence might be used to improve investment decisions.

Pierre Sarrau, chief investment officer of multi-asset strategies at BlackRock, says the company is talking to a UK-based Google company, adding that it is a "very exciting joint venture".

It is unclear which Google company that might be, but the internet giant last year acquired London-based DeepMind Technologies, which specialises in artificial intelligence - an area of particular interest to BlackRock.

DeepMind, founded in 2010 by Demis Hassabis, aims to combine "the best techniques from machine learning and systems neuroscience to build powerful general-purpose learning algorithms". Machine learning tries to create algorithms that predict future events using current data.

Asked about a possible partnership with Google DeepMind, a spokesperson for BlackRock says: "We use big data and machine-learning investment techniques across many strategies today. We are excited about the potential they offer."

Google declined to comment.

Earlier this year BlackRock hired Bill MacCartney, a former Google scientist, to help it apply machine learning to quantitative investment strategies.

Mr MacCartney works with the US fund house's global equities research team from its base in Silicon Valley.

Mr Sarrau says: "[The Silicon Valley hub is focused on] building tools to help us gather information from big data."

Several asset managers, including Amundi, BNY Mellon, State Street and NN Partners, have expressed an interest in analysing large volumes of data.

In a paper published in May 2015, BNY Mellon said machine learning could be used to identify "correlations and patterns" that "cannot be observed manually or even with traditional analytical techniques that require significant human guidance".

"There is a requirement for machines and data to link together initially," the paper adds.

Asset management houses are already making use of the increased amount of data available to them through social media sites.

BlackRock said last week that it is using data gathered from both Google and Twitter, the microblogging site, to help it formulate investment decisions. NN Investment Partners says it is also doing this.

Speaking at a fund market conference earlier this year, asset management executives warned that those who did not "get on board the big data train" would be "left behind".

Alongside investment processes, sales and operations were named as areas where data could help fund houses improve their business.

Although fund managers recognise that big data could provide valuable insights, many remain uncertain about how to apply vast data sets from sources as disparate as Twitter and Starbucks, the coffee chain, to their investment processes.

Nick Thomas, a partner at Baillie Gifford, the Edinburgh-based fund house, told FTfm: "We are absolutely fascinated by the power of big data and how it makes Google a fantastic business, and we are some of the biggest holders of [companies] such as Amazon [the ecommerce group] and Baidu [the Chinese search company], who are at the cutting edge of using data to build amazing internet businesses.

"But trying to fit some of those ideas into our investment process is difficult because we take a very long-term, slow approach to investing."

This article first appeared in Ignites Europe, an FT news service

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - MONEY
Biotech sell-off hurts specialist funds; INVESTING

By Judith Evans 
791 words
17 October 2015
Financial Times
FTFTA
London Ed1
03
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Woodford fund hit following Clinton attack on drug costs

Popular funds including Neil Woodford 's new investment trust have been hit by the sell-off in biotechnology stocks, which have dived almost 24 per cent in the past two months.

The 880m Woodford Patient Capital Trust , launched in April, shed 6.2 per cent of its net asset value over the past month due to declines by holdings such as Prothena and Northwest Biotherapeutics . Over three months, it is down 5.8 per cent, according to figures from FE Analytics.

The 634m Axa Framlington Biotech fund, an open-ended product held by many self-directed investors, meanwhile shed 12.2 per cent over the past month and 16.8 per cent over three months.

That followed years of steady rises, however. The Axa fund was up 280 per cent in the four years to July, meaning investors who entered the fund early will still have made substantial gains.

The biotech sell-off was sparked by comments from the US Democratic presidential hopeful Hillary Clinton, who said she would look to reform drug pricing if she is elected president in 2016.

Commentators including Janet Yellen , chair of the US Federal Reserve , had previously warned of stretched valuations in the fast-growing biotechnology sector, which looks to exploit biological processes to make drugs and other products.

Mr Woodford's head of investment communications, Mitchell Fraser-Jones, said in a trust update: "The portfolio's US stocks were among the weakest performers, as sentiment towards US biotech remained sour.

"Given the way that the sector has traded this year, with very strong performance in the first half, the sell-off is perhaps unsurprising.

"We have, however, been disappointed that stocks such as Prothena and Northwest Biotherapeutics have become so embroiled in it - the valuation attached to their shares is very far from the bubble-like levels that some . . . peers have reached."

The company has seen nothing to change the fundamental investment case for these stocks, he said.

"Although September was a disappointing month in performance terms, we retain absolute conviction in the long-term outlook for the trust," Mr Fraser-Jones added.

Patient Capital, which mainly invests in early-stage and early-growth companies - not exclusively in the biotech sector - is designed for investors who intend to commit money for at least five years.

It has shed 2.2 per cent of net asset value since its launch in April , but is still trading at an 8.8 per cent premium because of investors' faith in Mr Woodford's extended record.

Linden Thomson, manager of the Axa biotech fund , said her fund does not hold Valeant or Turing Pharmaceuticals , two of the companies most associated with drug price inflation, since she does not classify them as biotechnology companies.

Like Mr Woodford, she has made no significant changes to her portfolio - whose largest holdings are Celgene Corp and Gilead Sciences - as a result of recent market swings.

"We believe that fundamentals remain solid for this sector and maintain our forecast of 20 per cent growth over the next two years," said Ms Thomson. "In an environment concerned about growth, the biotech sector is one area which looks likely to continue to grow strongly through various market cycles."

Demographic trends and demand from countries outside the US and western Europe will continue to support the industry, she added, although it does face question marks over the sustainability of high drug prices, and must negotiate competition from generic drugs.

Rival healthcare funds have also taken a hit: Polar Capital's biotechnology fund shed 13.4 per cent over the three months to the end of September, while funds run by Candriam Investors Group and Pictet Asset Management were down 14.7 and 13.4 per cent respectively.

Among trusts, Frostrow's Biotech Growth Trust is down 23.5 per cent over three months, and the Worldwide Healthcare Trust, run by the same company, has shed 13.3 per cent of its value.

Polar Capital Global Healthcare Growth and Income declined 8.78 per cent over three months, while rival SV Life Sciences International Biotechnology Trust shed 16.4 per cent.

Jeremy Beckwith, director of manager research at Morningstar, said that biotech shares' fall was "a necessary correction after a phenomenal bull run". "It's had such a strong run and made some much money that the first time there was some bad news, people were bound to take some profits," he said.

"This is a high-risk, high-return sector. They [investors] need to understand how much they can afford to lose and whether they want to take on this sort of risk."

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - LIFE & ARTS
FT Diary

By Izabella Kaminska 
866 words
17 October 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
02
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

It's October in New York and the sun is blazing. I packed all the wrong clothes. But who can complain? There's nothing like autumn among the sun-soaked skyscrapers of Manhattan, with the pumpkins, the Halloween decorations and the leaves turning from green to orange to red.

I've arrived just in time for the finance-themed, intellectual marathon of the year: the FT pub quiz, organised by the New York division of FT Alphaville. They've set the questions, found the venue, invited the guests and, most importantly, bagged former Federal Reserve chairman Paul Volcker and the Financal Times' US managing editor Gillian Tett as quiz masters.

With hosts of that calibre, not to mention a prize list that includes drones and signed editions of Volcker's own 2012 "rule" restricting banks' ability to speculate (the toughest regulation to hit Wall Street since Sarbanes-Oxley in 2002), is it any wonder the event is so oversubscribed that we've had to hire a bouncer?

Paul Volcker arrives with Gillian Tett and is immediately escorted to the liquidity distribution zone - or bar. As we are introduced, I'm struck not just by how tall he is - 6ft 7in - but also by his charm and humour. At which point Tett remarks that chairman Volcker, who contributed questions of his own for the quiz, has stumbled across errors in the other sections. So much for Google, eh? A more stringent internet search quickly confirms - unsurprisingly - that he is right. We've made a factual error regarding the unemployment rate and inflation. Frantic question-restructuring follows.

"So, when will the Fed be raising rates?" a colleague asks him, cheekily.

"Not before 2056!" quips Volcker.

He's keener to talk about how the quiz will be a bridging of knowledge gaps. There's his knowledge of finance and our technological insight on social media and modern telecoms devices. Volcker calls this a fair exchange, though I suspect he is overegging his ignorance. When I mention bitcoin, he defines the mechanics that underpin the system in two short sentences. After the quiz starts, it soon becomes clear Volcker is probably as big an icon in financial circles as Kim Kardashian is on Instagram. Neither is he a social media newbie. On the sidelines, a queue begins to form for Volcker selfies.

While I'm in town I've arranged to meet financial crime author and writer Jeffrey Robinson for lunch at what he claims is a proper New York institution: Katz's Delicatessen on East Houston Street, best known as the setting for that cinematic moment when Sally met Harry to give him some much-needed advice on women. We chat about Robinson's new projects, his time cruising with the City of London police and celery-tonic soda, while chomping on pastrami sandwiches stacked 15cm high. Then Robinson insists on taking me to the Strand Bookstore on Broadway and its rare editions and crime aisles, where I clock his book and insist he signs it. Before we part, we exchange gifts - for me, a mug; for him, a book by Thomas Levenson called Newton and the Counterfeiter , about when the man who discovered gravity was offered a job as warden of the Royal Mint.

My day meeting authors with a penchant for shadowy areas of finance isn't over. I've arranged to meet the quant of all quants, Emanuel Derman, author of My Life as a Quant and Models Behaving Badly , for dinner in an Indian restaurant by Columbus Circle.

I can't wait to get his perspective on machine learning, volatility and feedback loops and Derman doesn't disappoint. His is a unique mind but one grounded in reality - what you'd expect from a former physicist. The conversation turns to Chappie , the 2015 AI film based in Johannesburg, which I recommend he - as a South African quant - should watch, especially after I discover he's a fan of Die Antwoord, the rap-rave duo who feature in the movie.

New York Comic Con sold outmonthsago but I am lucky to have wangled a press invite from Felix Salmon, editor at the multimedia network Fusion, to his panel on "The Amazing Economics of Star Trek ", featuring economists Paul Krugman and Brad DeLong. The panel also features Manu Saadia, author of the new book Trekonomics .

We quickly learn that the post-scarcity environment of Star Trek - where anything can be produced with replicators, and money doesn't exist - is probably not a utopian state. In fact, the panellists emphasise that the highly meritocratic society is likely to be highly unequal. For example, we, the viewers, only see the adventures of the top 1 per cent of the top 1 per cent of the top 1 per cent.

I ask the panellists whether the Fed will have raised rates by the 24th century? No one dares to answer - presumably because there is no money by then, and thus no interest rates?

At least I'm left with a workable forecast. Somewhere between 2056 and star date 2364 seems likely.

Izabella Kaminska is a reporter for FT Alphaville

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - MONEY
Highlights from this week's investors chronicle

By Jonas Crosland, Theron Mohamed and Harriet Russell 
865 words
17 October 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

BUY

Banks

Barclays (BARC)

Shares in Barclays fell nearly 3 per cent this week, and at 249p trade 14 per cent below this year's peak

Former JPMorgan investment banker Jes Staley is expected to be appointed chief executive of Barclays in the next few weeks. Mr Staley will be stepping into the shoes of former chief executive and retail banker Antony Jenkins who was sacked less than three months after John MacFarlane took over as chairman. Mr MacFarlane is expected to maintain a hands-on style of management even after relinquishing his interim executive role and going back to being chairman.

Before joining US hedge fund Blue Mountain Capital in 2013, Mr Staley spent 30 years at JPMorgan, and rose to be head of its investment bank. His expected appointment raises questions about the bank's direction, and whether it will steer more towards the investment banking side than its retail operations. Outwardly, this need not be a cause for alarm; the investment bank boosted pre-tax profit by more than a third to 1.4bn in the first half of this year, but it was this side that suffered the worst as a result of the economic crash.

Barclays is in the process of a significant restructuring of its operations, having shed over 10 per cent of its workforce and a quarter of the assets on its balance sheet. UK banks are also having to go through a protracted scheme whereby retail banking is ringfenced from investment banking activities to protect taxpayers from having to bail out a bank again. Barclays faces a tough challenge because of the size of its investment bank arm which grew with the acquisition of the US operation formerly run by Lehman Brothers. Jonas Crosland

BUY

Media

Next Fifteen Comms (NFC)

Next Fifteen shares have risen 7 per cent since July. They trade on 13 times forecast earnings, which looks cheap

Few marketing and media relations agencies count Google, Amazon and Facebook among their clients; Next Fifteen Communications added Intel and Airbnb to its impressive roster in the six months to the end of July, as brisk demand for its data analytics, software and digital communications services fuelled a 31 per cent rise in operating profit to 7.2m.

Next Fifteen has been quick to embrace new ways of getting clients' messages across, including analysing data to hone campaigns and using new channels such as social media.

These efforts underpinned organic sales growth of a tenth in the US as Outcast, Beyond and other agencies won work.

The group also shelled out 2.4m to acquire three UK agencies and continued to streamline its non-US overseas operations.

This process, which has involved pruning the client base, cut constant-currency revenue by 9 per cent in Europe, the Middle East and Africa, but helped more than double the operating margin in Asia Pacific to 13.2 per cent.

The company's management remains dedicated to digital innovation. Its Agent3 unit recently built a software platform that provides insights into customers for the sales and marketing departments of large companies. Next Fifteen plans to boost investment in product development and sales support here.

Broker Investec Securities expects pre-tax profit of 15.6m for the year to January 2016, giving earnings per share of 15.3p. Theron Mohamed

HOLD

General retailers

N Brown (BWNG)

Broker Peel Hunt thinks it could be "a stretch" for the retailer to meet current forecasts

Shares in retailer N Brown trade on a fairly undemanding forward price/earnings ratio of 14, and the dividend yield is generous, but investors have been burnt before on this one.

The share price rose by 7 per cent after the group reported first-half results that were better than expected. However, expectations were low as a result of extensive restructuring under way at the company and last year's profit warning. Even on an adjusted basis, pre-tax profits for the half-year to August 29 fell 16 per cent to 35m.

Chief executive Angela Spindler told investors to expect a stronger finish to the current financial year. That's because exceptional costs associated with the revamp - which involved 380 redundancies and the closure of 18 clearance stores in the first half, costing nearly 15m - should fall to between 2m and 3m in the second half.

As the operational transformation from a catalogue-led business to a digital retailer continues, N Brown is also changing the way it reports its financial results. For the first time, the group split its revenues into "product" and "financial services". The former grew 6.1 per cent during the first six months as digital sales took off, while the latter - which tracks the money N Brown makes on the credit it offers customers - dipped 0.4 per cent. Overall, like-for-like sales grew 3.1 per cent.

Peel Hunt expects pre-tax profits of 86.6m for the current financial year, giving earnings per share of 24.6p, compared with 86.2m and 21p for the year ended February 2015. Harriet Russell

Investors Chronicle: Barclays, Next Fifteen, N Brown and high-growth Aim shares

Investors Chronicle reporters 
2,057 words
16 October 2015
17:30
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Buy: Barclays (BARC)

Shares in Barclays fell nearly 3 per cent on the news of Mr Staley s expected appointment, and at 249p trade 14 per cent below this year s peak, just after the bank announced a 25 per cent rise in interim pre-tax profit, writes Jonas Crosland.

Former JPMorgan investment banker Jes Staley is expected to be appointed chief executive of Barclays in the next few weeks. Mr Staley will be stepping into the shoes of former chief executive and retail banker Antony Jenkins who was sacked less than three months after John MacFarlane took over as chairman. Mr MacFarlane is expected to maintain a hands-on style of management even after relinquishing his interim executive role and going back to being chairman.

Before joining US hedge fund Blue Mountain Capital in 2013, Mr Staley spent 30 years at JPMorgan, and rose to be head of its investment bank. His expected appointment raises questions about the bank s strategic direction, and whether the emphasis will steer more towards the investment banking side rather than its retail operations. Outwardly, this need not be a cause for alarm; the investment bank boosted pre-tax profit by more than a third to 1.4bn in the first half of this year, but it was the investment banking side that suffered the worst as a result of the economic crash.

Barclays is in the process of a significant restructuring of its operations, having shed over 10 per cent of its workforce and a quarter of the assets on its balance sheet. UK banks are also having to go through a protracted scheme whereby retail banking is ringfenced from investment banking activities to protect taxpayers from ever having to foot the bill of bailing out a bank again. Barclays faces a particularly tough challenge because of the size of its investment banking arm which grew with the acquisition of the US operation formerly run by Lehman Brothers.

Buy: Next Fifteen (NFC)

Next Fifteen s shares have risen 7 per cent since July. They trade on 13 times forecast earnings, which looks cheap given the group s blue-chip clients, writes Theron Mohamed.

Few marketing and media relations agencies count Google, Amazon and Facebook among their clients; Next Fifteen Communications added Intel and Airbnb to its impressive roster in the six months to end-July, as brisk demand for its data analytics, software and digital communications services fuelled a 31 per cent rise in operating profit to 7.2m.

Next Fifteen has been quick to embrace new ways of getting clients  messages across, including analysing data to hone campaigns and using new channels such as social media. These efforts underpinned organic sales growth of a tenth in the US as Outcast, Beyond and other agencies won work.

The group also shelled out 2.4m to acquire three UK agencies and continued to streamline its non-US overseas operations. This process, which has involved pruning the client base, cut constant-currency revenue by 9 per cent in Europe, the Middle East and Africa, but helped more than double the operating margin in Asia Pacific to 13.2 per cent.

Management remains dedicated to digital innovation. Its Agent3 unit recently built a software platform that provides insights into customers for the sales and marketing departments of large companies. Next Fifteen plans to boost investment in product development and sales support here.

Broker Investec Securities expects pre-tax profit of 15.6m for the year to January 2016, giving earnings per share of 15.3p.

Hold: N Brown (BWNG)

Management is bullish on the second half, but Peel Hunt thinks it could be  a stretch  for the company to meet current forecasts, writes Harriet Russell.

Shares in retailer N Brown rose by 7 per cent after the group reported first-half results that were better than expected. However, expectations were low as a result of the extensive restructuring effort still under way at the company and last year s shock profit warning. Even on an adjusted basis, pre-tax profits for the half-year to August 29 fell 16 per cent to 35m.

The shares trade on a fairly undemanding forward price/earnings ratio of 14, and the dividend yield is generous, but investors have been burnt before on this one.

Chief executive Angela Spindler told investors to expect a stronger finish to the current financial year. That s because exceptional costs associated with the revamp   which involved 380 redundancies and the closure of 18 clearance stores in the first half, costing nearly 15m   should fall to between 2m and 3m in the second half.

As the operational transformation from a catalogue-led business to a digital retailer continues, N Brown is also changing the way it reports its financial results.

For the first time, the group split its revenues into  product  and  financial services . The former grew 6.1 per cent during the first six months as digital sales took off, while the latter   which tracks the money N Brown makes on the credit it offers customers   dipped 0.4 per cent. Overall, like-for-like sales grew 3.1 per cent.

Peel Hunt expects pre-tax profits of 86.6m for the current financial year, giving earnings per share of 24.6p, compared with 86.2m and 21p for the year ended February 2015.

Stock Screen: Cheap, High-Growth Aim Shares

The excitement the Alternative Investment Market (Aim) evokes in many investors often centres on the huge gains it is possible to make from finding the right go-to growth stock, writes Algy Hall.

My Cheap, High-Growth Aim Shares screen hit upon one such situation last year when it picked Trakm8. The shares delivered a flabbergasting total return of almost 250 per cent over the 12 months. Even after that amazing run, the shares can hardly be seen as expensive given that they make a reappearance among the screen s picks for this year, too.

There were actually nine shares from Aim that produced higher returns than Trakm8 over the 12 months, all of which considerably outflanked the biggest risers from the FTSE All-Share index

The success stories from last year s screen may present the case for Aim, but its failures also represent a case for the opposition. Among the big fallers from the 19 shares selected were a number of companies hit by the falling oil price and reduced capital expenditure by the industry.

The cumulative total return from the screen now stands at 4.8 per cent over the two years I ve run it, compared with a negative 5.8 per cent from the index. If I factor in a 2 per cent charge to account for the high dealing costs associated with small-caps, the cumulative total return drops to 2.7 per cent.

Relative to the market, the screen s performance can be said to be decent, but I feel it has yet to distinguish itself. That said, the methodology certainly seems capable of turning up some interesting situations   even if these do come accompanied by some less welcome investment ideas. The valuation criteria the screen uses is based on a cash and dividend-adjusted price-to-earnings-growth ratio, which I ve called a genuine value (GV) ratio. It is calculated as follows:

Enterprise-value-to-operating-profits ratio divided by dividend yield plus average forecast growth for the next two fiscal years

The rest of the criteria for the screen centre on a hunt for high forecast earnings growth, though growth that looks suspiciously high (an average of over 100 per cent for the next two financial years) is ignored. Broker forecasts for small-caps can be very subjective and the screen also seeks a bit of comfort from a sound balance sheet. Where earnings growth has been achieved historically, the screen wants to see that it is supported by sales growth. The full screening criteria are:

%Ï Average forecast earnings per share (EPS) growth rate over the next two financial years of more than 20 per cent but less than 100 per cent.

%Ï Three-year revenue compound annual growth rate (CAGR) equal to at least half three-year EPS CAGR.

%Ï A genuine value ratio of less than the median average.

%Ï Forward next 12-month price/earnings ratio outside the most expensive quarter of stocks screened.

%Ï Net debt/cash profit (Ebitda) of less than 2.5 times.

%Ï A current ratio (current assets/current liabilities) of more than 1.

This year, 17 shares were highlighted by the screen. I ve taken a closer look at the shares showing the best momentum, offering the highest dividend yield:

32 Red   The UK s new point-of-consumption gambling tax has been a blow to online gambling companies. Unlike peers, 32 Red held off on heavy marketing ahead of the tax s introduction, which has left it with plenty of cash to recruit new players now the tax has been ushered in. Its efforts have been made all the more effective because many rivals have reined in their marketing spending.

Impressively, 32 Red has been managing to recruit new users without experiencing any serious deterioration in yield per user, and first-half casino revenues were 21 per cent ahead. The company, which is principally focused on the UK, is also seeing an improved performance from its Italian business which reported a 61 per cent revenue increase. The integration of Roxy Palace, acquired this year, is going well.

Belvoir   Lettings-focused estate agency chain Belvoir operates a franchise model. The beauty of a good franchise operation for shareholders is that a company can operate a large network of income-generating units with relatively minimal overheads   just the central costs needed to support franchisees. That means there s the potential for a large amount of earnings to be paid out as dividends, which is what Belvoir has been doing.

But while this means Belvoir s dividend payout is high, the dividend cover is slim. Profit growth is expected to boost cover, though, with broker Cantor Fitzgerald forecasting that earnings will be 1.3 times the level of the payout in 2016. And while last year s net operating cash inflow of 250,000 didn t come close to covering the 1.6m paid out as dividends, a solid balance sheet provides some reassurance, with Cantor forecasting that net debt will represent less than half cash profits in 2016.

During its second half, the company has bought two letting agencies in the Midlands which has helped boost earnings growth expectations as well as helping advance its  multi-brand  strategy. The equity-funded deals are expected to mean Belvoir ends the year with 213 outlets, compared with 166 at the halfway stage.

Swallowfield   A new strategic plan means cosmetics manufacturer Swallowfield is showing early signs of promise and could drive substantial earnings growth over coming years. Indeed, broker N+1 Singer thinks in the best-case scenario the company could generate compound annual earnings growth in the three years to June 2018 of over 50 per cent taking EPS to 22p, which includes the tax benefits of past losses. That compares with 2015 EPS of 6.5p. For now, the broker s official 2018 forecast is 19.4p.

Forecasting so far out is necessarily a matter of extreme conjecture, but Swallowfield s full-year results in September provided encouragement despite the negative impact of the euro s weakness. Plans to improve efficiency have helped the group put 230,000 worth of cost savings in train and further savings of 150,000 have been identified. The company also reported two contract wins in global brands, which helped validate increased focus on products where it has a strong advantage and sees good growth. There were also encouraging signs on the potential take-up of the owned brands the group has been developing and is getting ready to roll out.

Management s confidence in prospects was underlined by the decision to reinstate a dividend at 2p. Strong dividend growth is forecast with N+1 pencilling in a payment of 3p for the current financial year rising to 5p in 2018, which would be equivalent to a 3.6 per cent yield. The potential looks undeniably exciting.

COMPANIES
Eager-to-lend banks welcome new ways to filter creditworthy clients; Financials: Debt risks

By Ben McLannahan in New York 
627 words
16 October 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Analytics groups are using increasingly creative methods to assess loan applications

The US's middle classes are drenched in debt and demand is slowing. So how do banks build profits? By bending the rules.

Two of the top credit analytics companies are exploring new ways of assessing consumers' ability to handle loans, turning away from traditional sources of data such as credit cards and car loans to scour phone and utility bills, change-of-address records and information drawn from DVD clubs and suppliers of rent-to-own furniture.

The efforts could open up new classes of customer for the big US banks , and come as lenders struggle to generate steady profits in an environment of tightened regulation and ultra-low interest rates. In third-quarter results this week, the US's three biggest banks - Wells Fargo, Bank of America and JPMorgan Chase - have reported year-on-year declines in quarterly revenue .

One of the companies, Fico, has been working on a pilot project with a dozen US credit card companies, and now claims to have developed reliable ways to price loans to millions of people who have historically been off the grid.

Will Lansing , chief executive of the San Jose, California-based company, said Fico was increasingly looking at data on a spectrum: with credit card repayment history at one end - the most reliable guide to creditworthiness - and at the other, information volunteered on social media platforms such as Facebook.

"If you look at how many times a person says 'wasted' in their profile, it has some value in predicting whether they're going to repay their debt," he said. "It's not much, but it's more than zero."

Credit cards and car loans are likely to be among the bright spots in the US banks' third-quarter figures, with much of the growth driven by segments considered subprime . But mortgages and personal loans should be patchier, suggesting that many consumers have borrowed about as much as they can.

Aggregate household debt balances were unchanged in the second quarter at $11.85tn, according to the New York Federal Reserve, and remain more than 6 per cent below the pre-Lehman peak.

TransUnion, another credit-analytics group, has embarked on a project to tap alternative data sources to help people with marred records get better terms.

Both Fico and TransUnion say they are responding to demand from their ultimate customers, the banks, which worry that by declining people without traditional credit scores they are turning away potential sources of profit.

"The market was absolutely hungry for a solution," said Jim Wehmann, executive vice-president for scores at Fico.

Fico, which led the practice of custom scoring in the 1950s and whose scores are now used in about 90 per cent of credit decisions in the US, is pursuing its project in partnership with Equifax, one of the top three credit-reporting bureaux, and LexisNexis Risk Solutions.

The new model gives high marks to people who have regularly paid bills, and have not moved around enough to suggest problems paying rent.

Some of the new credit-eligible customers are new to the country, some are young - and some have simply not borrowed enough to generate traditional files. But the alternative data suggest that many are a good credit risk. "We can now score the previously unscoreable," said Mr Wehmann.

Mike Mondelli, Chicago-based senior vice-president of alternative data services at TransUnion, said a broader approach to scoring could lead to cheaper loans for 23m consumers.

From the banks' point of view, he said, it is "good for adding more customers at an acceptable risk rate".

Markets
AIG Likes That New Underwriter Has No Underwriting Training; Madhu Tadikonda joined AIG in 2013 as part of its  science team,  which now tops 150 employees

By Leslie Scism 
555 words
16 October 2015
01:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

American International Group Inc.'s new chief underwriter for its big business-insurance unit doesn't have underwriting training, and that is exactly what the company likes about him.

It would seem like just another job announcement when the New York company Thursday named a new chief underwriting officer for its big business-insurance unit, but the appointment of data scientist Madhu Tadikonda signifies sweeping change in the insurance industry.

AIG says it isn't getting rid of traditional underwriters, but instead wants to augment their work with data science. Underwriters evaluate the risks of insuring a particular client or entity, determining the likelihood and potential for claims over the life of the policy.

The new chief underwriter is a 43-year-old Princeton University graduate who studied philosophy and later earned an M.B.A. from Stanford University. He worked previously at a consulting firm and in venture capital helping credit-card companies and retailers with computer modeling techniques for marketing and sales, among other assignments.

Mr. Tadikonda joined AIG in 2013 as part of a  science team" committed to helping the global insurer apply computer-analytical techniques to its insurance business.

AIG Commercial Insurance Chief Executive John Doyle said that Mr. Tadikonda is unusual for the new assignment because he hadn't trained for years as an underwriter, but that is what he wanted.

 I appointed him because of his unique background," he said in an interview.  I want him to challenge some of the conventional ways we've done things in the past, to look at our data assets in a different more creative way. We need people in key leadership roles that can help the team more broadly understand how we can do things in a different and better way."

AIG's move  signifies that insurance is  in a new era, where the importance of sophisticated data analysis is becoming increasingly apparent," said Robert Hartwig, president of trade group Insurance Information Institute.

Across the insurance industry some companies still rely almost exclusively on underwriters' gut instincts. But AIG Chief Executive Peter Hancock has for several years championed use of science, data and analytics to assess risk. In late 2011, he hired a  Chief Science Officer" and the science team now tops 150 employees.

Mr. Tadikonda, who worked alongside underwriters in his job on the science team, said that computer models won't replace human judgment.

 People feel very comfortable popping up over their cubicle and asking the three people they sit next to whether they've seen an account like this or how they handled a situation," he said in an interview.  The models crystallize or summarize that kind of experience across all of AIG's underwriters over a long period of time."

The models  aren't great" for all cases or conditions, so  that is where the human perspective is valuable," he added.

Mr. Tadikonda replaces Tim Carter, who moves to another role at AIG. Mr. Carter had a more-traditional background: years of working up the ranks of underwriting departments, after earning a mathematics degree and becoming an actuary.

Write to Leslie Scism at "mailto:leslie.scism@wsj.com"

Biotech sell-off hurts Woodford trust and specialist funds

Judith Evans 
798 words
15 October 2015
12:13
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Popular funds including Neil Woodford s new investment trust have been hit by the sell-off in biotechnology stocks, which have dived almost 24 per cent in the past two months.

The 880m Woodford Patient Capital Trust, launched in April, shed 6.2 per cent of its net asset value over the past month due to declines by holdings such as Prothena and Northwest Biotherapeutics. Over three months, it is down 5.8 per cent, according to figures from FE Analytics.

The 634m Axa Framlington Biotech fund, an open-ended product held by many self-directed investors, meanwhile shed 12.2 per cent over the past month and 16.8 per cent over three months.

That followed years of steady rises, however. The Axa fund was up 280 per cent in the four years to July, meaning investors who entered the fund early will still have made substantial gains.

The biotech sell-off was sparked by comments from the US Democratic presidential hopeful Hillary Clinton, who said she would look to reform drug pricing if she is elected president in 2016.

Commentators including Janet Yellen, chair of the US Federal Reserve, had previously warned of stretched valuations in the fast-growing biotechnology sector, which looks to exploit biological processes to make drugs and other products.

Mr Woodford s head of investment communications, Mitchell Fraser-Jones, said in a trust update:  The portfolio s US stocks were among the weakest performers, as sentiment towards US biotech remained sour.

 Given the way that the sector has traded this year, with very strong performance in the first half, the sell-off is perhaps unsurprising.

 We have, however, been disappointed that stocks such as Prothena and Northwest Biotherapeutics have become so embroiled in it   the valuation attached to their shares is very far from the bubble-like levels that some 	. 	. 	. 	peers have reached. 

The company has seen nothing to change the fundamental investment case for these stocks, he said.

 Although September was a disappointing month in performance terms, we retain absolute conviction in the long-term outlook for the trust,  Mr Fraser-Jones added.

Patient Capital, which mainly invests in early-stage and early-growth companies   not exclusively in the biotech sector   is designed for investors who intend to commit their money for at least five years.

It has shed 2.2 per cent of net asset value since its launch in April, but is still trading at an 8.8 per cent premium because of investors  faith in Mr Woodford s extended record.

Linden Thomson, manager of the Axa biotech fund, said her fund does not hold Valeant or Turing Pharmaceuticals, two of the companies most associated with drug price inflation, since she does not classify them as biotechnology companies.

Like Mr Woodford, she has made no significant changes to her portfolio   whose largest holdings are Celgene Corp and Gilead Sciences   as a result of recent market swings.

 We believe that fundamentals remain solid for this sector and maintain our forecast of 20 per cent growth over the next two years,  said Ms Thomson.

 In an environment concerned about growth, the biotech sector is one area which looks likely to continue to grow strongly through various market cycles. 

Demographic trends and demand from countries outside the US and western Europe will continue to support the industry, she added, although it does face question marks over the sustainability of high drug prices, and must negotiate competition from generic drugs.

Rival healthcare funds have also taken a hit: Polar Capital s biotechnology fund shed 13.4 per cent over the three months to the end of September, while biotech funds run by Candriam Investors Group and Pictet Asset Management were down 14.7 and 13.4 per cent respectively.

Among trusts, Frostrow s Biotech Growth Trust is down 23.5 per cent over three months, and the Worldwide Healthcare Trust, run by the same company, has shed 13.3 per cent of its value.

Polar Capital Global Healthcare Growth and Income declined 8.78 per cent over three months, while rival SV Life Sciences International Biotechnology Trust shed 16.4 per cent.

Jeremy Beckwith, director of manager research at Morningstar, said that biotech shares  fall was  a necessary correction after a phenomenal bull run .

 It s had such a strong run and made some much money that the first time there was some bad news, people were bound to take some profits,  he said.

The role of biotech in an individual investor s portfolio should depend on their risk appetite, he added.  This is a high-risk, high-return sector. They need to understand how much they can afford to lose and whether they want to take on this sort of risk.

pict011.png

Start-up threat to creaking banks

Patrick Jenkins 
1,242 words
14 October 2015
06:03
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

To judge by her CV, Anne Boden is an old-style banker to the core. She has a conventional career behind her, spanning stints at Lloyds, Standard Chartered, UBS and Royal Bank of Scotland, culminating as chief operating officer of Allied Irish.

Now, however, Ms Boden has moved into the vanguard of the digital revolution. As chief executive of "http://starlingbank.co.uk/", a London-based start-up, she believes the future of high-street banking is not on the high street at all, but on mobile-only platforms such as the one she is midway through setting up.

 Big banks are far too complicated. All their branches and products and legacy systems are very inefficient,  Ms Boden says.

And she is not alone in holding such views. Across the financial services sector, but particularly in banking, start-ups are endeavouring to shake up a staid industry.

Three connected complications make this a rich vein to tap. In the boom times of the late 1990s and early 2000s, many banks failed to invest in technology, preferring simply to ride the wave and maximise profits. During the financial crisis, meanwhile, there was no time to think of tech investment. And in the post-crisis period there has been no money. The net result across much of the world is a banking system that is creaking at the joints.

The industry has been distracted for years, says Piyush Gupta, chief executive of DBS, Singapore s biggest lender.  Collectively, we have taken our eyes off Net 2.0. 

Groups that expanded aggressively through acquisition have some of the biggest challenges thanks to a common failure to integrate multiple IT systems. Kartik Ramakrishnan, senior vice-president at Capgemini, a consultancy, says the task in hand is vast.

 The financial services industry is one of the biggest spenders on technology,  he says.  However, the majority of this spend is on maintenance activities, largely due to the investment required to keep legacy systems operational. 

Royal Bank of Scotland, which led a three-way  ¬71bn "http://ftalphaville.ft.com/tag/abn-amro/page/2/" just before the financial crisis, has become synonymous with IT failures. After "http://www.rbs.com/news/2014/11/rbs-reaches-it-incident-settlement.html" in the summer of 2012 it took weeks to clear a backlog of 100m unprocessed transactions that froze customers out of their accounts.

Despite a 750m programme of investment, RBS was hit by another embarrassing glitch this summer.

Some of the biggest banks with the greatest need to make amends for past under-investment are now spending billions of dollars a year   not just to maintain old systems, but to try to upgrade them at last.

 There is an urgent need for the traditional players to acknowledge the presence of new digital challengers,  says Mr Ramakrishnan.

 [Banks need] to develop more agile, responsive IT to support faster time to market, better product innovation and improved customer service. 

DBS of Singapore is among the banks convinced it still has time to make amends. Next-generation technology is now  front and centre of our priorities , says Mr Gupta, who adds that the timescale for getting it done is limited.

 If you don t get the digital transformation right in the next five years, you will be history,  he says.

As with other bank bosses, Mr Gupta is alarmed at the inroads made into traditional banking territory by non-banks, particularly technological behemoths, from Alibaba in China to Apple across much of the rest of the world.

Their expansion into payment services is particularly worrying for the banks because it directly challenges the banks  relationships with their customers.

Other core banking activities   such as lending   are under attack from technology start-ups, particularly so-called peer-to-peer (P2P) lenders.

By using sophisticated software to match people or companies with money to lend to those who want to borrow, they can often offer loans more cheaply and cut out the middlemen bankers.  We are steadily taking their customers,  says Giles Andrews, co-founder of Zopa, the world s oldest P2P lender, who adds:  We are doubling our market share every year. 

A handful of initiatives pose similar threats to other areas of the traditional financial services market.

In asset management, for example, Alibaba has attracted vast inflows across China, thanks to the combined power of its technology and its dominant brand name.

In insurance, a patchwork of initiatives has spread across broking and analytics.

It is in banking services, however, that the greatest changes are afoot. Here, technology companies, large and small, have been helped in their competitive efforts by the increasing regulatory constraints imposed on traditional banks in the aftermath of the 2008 crisis.

While banks have been forced to accept tougher regulatory capital requirements, making much of their core lending more expensive, challengers from outside banking are only lightly regulated.

In some countries, politicians have even gone out of their way to favour non-banks, conscious that there are votes to be won in making life harder for the kinds of banking institutions that were so closely identified with the financial crisis.

In the UK, for example, P2P lending is about to be given the tax breaks and government imprimatur of an Isa, a tax-efficient savings vehicle. At the same time, a new 8 per cent bank supertax has been announced for old-style banks.

The banking industry is not taking the assault lying down. With the trauma of the crisis, and much of the expense of post-crisis regulation now absorbed, the stronger banks have not only begun to invest heavily in unseen back-office IT. There is also a good deal of customer-facing tech investment, ensuring that some banks, at least, are keeping pace with upstart challengers.

A number of models are emerging. Spanish lenders BBVA and Santander have each set up pools of funding to buy up tech-based firms. BBVA even went so far as to acquire Simple, one of the more eye-catching of the US start-up lenders. Meanwhile, BNP Paribas has established Hello, a mobile-focused lender operating across a large chunk of the eurozone.

For Ms Boden at Starling, such initiatives miss the point because, in most cases, the high-tech front ends of the new style still rely on old bank infrastructure.

Of her own enterprise she says:  We re different. We re going to be able to offer free current accounts without subsidy from other products.

 That s because we don t have the big network overheads of branches and legacy systems. 

If she is right, then perhaps the greatest potential for a more modern form of banking may lie in emerging markets, where old-style infrastructure was never built.

Across Africa, for example, payment services using basic mobiles rather than smartphones are already widely available. In such markets, tech-savvy start-up lenders could do very well indeed.

Many believe, however, that the current phase of traditional financial services being challenged by upstart technology companies will morph into a new world order, in which the best of the old operators integrate the latest technology, either under their own steam or via acquisition.

 The financial services industry is at a crossroads,  says Mr Ramakrishnan.  But we firmly believe that some firms will retake the initiative and challenge the new digital challengers at their own game.

 Indeed, we may see symbiotic relationships emerge, where both established firms and challengers come together to create a mutually beneficial partnership.

FT REPORT - THE CONNECTED BUSINESS
How to make all passwords vanish in a heartbeat

By Hannah Kuchler 
859 words
14 October 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys CBU1
04
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Cyber security Standard access codes could soon become a thing of the past, reports Hannah Kuchler

Wearables have been used to track our steps, deliver emails to our wrists and monitor our sun exposure. But now connected devices on our bodies could help us access online banking systems.

Nymi, taken from the Greek suffix -onym for name, is experimenting with a system that lets people prove who they are with their heartbeat. Halifax, part of UK bank Lloyds, Canada's RBC and MasterCard are partners.

Everyone's heartbeat is individual to them, so by wearing a wristband that transmits the signal to the device being used for online banking, or even a till at a checkout, you can prove your identity.

Shawn Chance, vice-president of marketing and business development at Nymi, says the company aims to "make all the passwords go away". "The idea of a password being used for security is almost eroded," he says. He adds people tend to keep a note of them in insecure places.

Banks want to better secure their systems from hackers, who like latter-day bank robbers are attracted to the money in their - now digital - vaults. There are also more sophisticated nation state attackers who desire not only cash, but data and seek to make a political impact by targeting an adversary's most prestigious financial institutions.

Many banks are cautious about putting any extra burden on consumers, already stressed by remembering codes and passwords, and who prioritise convenient and easy access to their money over security.

Even Nymi is unlikely to be accepted as a wearable given its limited use. "We don't have any illusions that, in order for the device to be useful for an everyday person, it has to do a lot more than get you into online banking," Mr Chance says. So the company is making wristbands for employees that allow or restrict access to parts of the office or IT network as an additional feature.

Banking is often held up as the sector that has devoted great amounts of time and resources to cyber security. But the threat facing institutions is also changing fast, forcing them to regularly seek out new solutions.

Tammy Moskites, chief information security officer at Venafi, a digital security company that works with four of the top five US banks, says the types of attacks have changed since she worked in information security for banks six years ago.

In the past, she says: "What we found . . . was attacks were on online banking, wire transfers, man-in-the-middle phishing-type attacks, about intercepting or redirecting money, sending it to Nigeria, rather than to payroll."

Now attackers are using techniques that are harder to detect: "The nation state type of attack is just stealing this information to show they can do it . . . the money is usually redirected to support the crazies of the world, terrorists, people causing havoc."

But as banks have moved towards greater encryption in the effort to keep data safe - even if it is stolen - they have created another problem. They cannot see what is moving in and out of their networks because it is encrypted.

"Banks, as well as other companies in general, encrypt more and more of their data," Ms Moskites says. "In the past, it was just the most critical information, things required by regulators, now they are going above and beyond that. However, when it is leaving their environment, the tools notoriously don't see that data, [making banks blind] to potential problems."

Some banks are trying to address this by making sure they know what is encrypted and why and are looking at ways to decrypt it briefly as it leaves the network.

Mark Nicholson, chief operating officer at Deloitte cyber security practice Vigilant, says banks are increasingly turning to analytics to try to identify who is moving money without placing a burden on their customers.

"What we're seeing is an unwillingness to implement technologies which would cause friction to the consumer," he says. "So they are using analytics on transactions to understand if certain types of transaction look anomalous - from a profile and history of the times of day you usually transact, from which general internet provider address, browser, machine details, etc."

US-based Vasco Data Security works with more than half of the world's banks to prevent account takeover and transaction tampering for online and mobile banking. John Gunn, a vice-president at Vasco, says US consumers are more focused on convenience and service than security because they have no lasting exposure to losses from hacking or fraud. "Here in the US, consumers don't care as much because their status as a victim is quickly and painlessly alleviated by their bank, and usually within 24 hours," he says.

As a result, Vasco has to focus on verifying customers' identities discreetly and it has about 20 ways to authenticate a mobile banking customer. "Most of them are done in the background, without the user even knowing they are happening," Mr Gunn says.

FT REPORT - THE CONNECTED BUSINESS
Start-up threat to creaking banks

By Patrick Jenkins 
1,262 words
14 October 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys CBU1
01
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Lenders are alarmed by the inroads being made into their territory, writes Patrick Jenkins

To judge by her CV, Anne Boden is an old-style banker to the core. She has a conventional career behind her, spanning stints at Lloyds, Standard Chartered, UBS and Royal Bank of Scotland, culminating as chief operating officer of Allied Irish.

Now, however, Ms Boden has moved into the vanguard of the digital revolution. As chief executive of Starling Bank , a London-based start-up, she believes the future of high-street banking is not on the high street at all, but on mobile-only platforms such as the one she is midway through setting up.

"Big banks are far too complicated. All their branches and products and legacy systems are very inefficient," Ms Boden says.

And she is not alone in holding such views. Across the financial services sector, but particularly in banking, start-ups are endeavouring to shake up a staid industry.

Three connected complications make this a rich vein to tap. In the boom times of the late 1990s and early 2000s, many banks failed to invest in technology, preferring simply to ride the wave and maximise profits. During the financial crisis, meanwhile, there was no time to think of tech investment. And in the post-crisis period there has been no money. The net result across much of the world is a banking system that is creaking at the joints.

The industry has been distracted for years, says Piyush Gupta, chief executive of DBS, Singapore's biggest lender. "Collectively, we have taken our eyes off Net 2.0."

Groups that expanded aggressively through acquisition have some of the biggest challenges thanks to a common failure to integrate multiple IT systems. Kartik Ramakrishnan, senior vice-president at Capgemini, a consultancy, says the task in hand is vast.

"The financial services industry is one of the biggest spenders on technology," he says. "However, the majority of this spend is on maintenance activities, largely due to the investment required to keep legacy systems operational."

Royal Bank of Scotland, which led a three-way  ¬71bn acquisition of ABN Amro just before the financial crisis, has become synonymous with IT failures. After RBS's systems fell down in the summer of 2012 it took weeks to clear a backlog of 100m unprocessed transactions that froze customers out of their accounts.

Despite a 750m programme of investment, RBS was hit by another embarrassing glitch this summer .

Some of the biggest banks with the greatest need to make amends for past under-investment are now spending billions of dollars a year - not just to maintain old systems, but to try to upgrade them at last.

"There is an urgent need for the traditional players to acknowledge the presence of new digital challengers," says Mr Ramakrishnan.

"[Banks need] to develop more agile, responsive IT to support faster time to market, better product innovation and improved customer service."

DBS of Singapore is among the banks convinced it still has time to make amends. Next-generation technology is now "front and centre of our priorities", says Mr Gupta, who adds that the timescale for getting it done is limited.

"If you don't get the digital transformation right in the next five years, you will be history," he says.

As with other bank bosses, Mr Gupta is alarmed at the inroads made into traditional banking territory by non-banks, particularly technological behemoths, from Alibaba in China to Apple across much of the rest of the world.

Their expansion into payment services is particularly worrying for the banks because it directly challenges the banks' relationships with their customers.

Other core banking activities - such as lending - are under attack from technology start-ups, particularly so-called peer-to-peer (P2P) lenders.

By using sophisticated software to match people or companies with money to lend to those who want to borrow, they can often offer loans more cheaply and cut out the middlemen bankers. "We are steadily taking their customers," says Giles Andrews, co-founder of Zopa, the world's oldest P2P lender, who adds: "We are doubling our market share every year."

A handful of initiatives pose similar threats to other areas of the traditional financial services market.

In asset management, for example, Alibaba has attracted vast inflows across China, thanks to the combined power of its technology and its dominant brand name.

In insurance, a patchwork of initiatives has spread across broking and analytics.

It is in banking services, however, that the greatest changes are afoot. Here, technology companies, large and small, have been helped in their competitive efforts by the increasing regulatory constraints imposed on traditional banks in the aftermath of the 2008 crisis.

While banks have been forced to accept tougher regulatory capital requirements, making much of their core lending more expensive, challengers from outside banking are only lightly regulated.

In some countries, politicians have even gone out of their way to favour non-banks, conscious that there are votes to be won in making life harder for the kinds of banking institutions that were so closely identified with the financial crisis.

In the UK, for example, P2P lending is about to be given the tax breaks and government imprimatur of an Isa, a tax-efficient savings vehicle. At the same time, a new 8 per cent bank supertax has been announced for old-style banks.

The banking industry is not taking the assault lying down. With the trauma of the crisis, and much of the expense of post-crisis regulation now absorbed, the stronger banks have not only begun to invest heavily in unseen back-office IT. There is also a good deal of customer-facing tech investment, ensuring that some banks, at least, are keeping pace with upstart challengers.

A number of models are emerging. Spanish lenders BBVA and Santander have each set up pools of funding to buy up tech-based firms. BBVA even went so far as to acquire Simple, one of the more eye-catching of the US start-up lenders. Meanwhile, BNP Paribas has established Hello, a mobile-focused lender operating across a large chunk of the eurozone.

For Ms Boden at Starling, such initiatives miss the point because, in most cases, the high-tech front ends of the new style still rely on old bank infrastructure.

Of her own enterprise she says: "We're different. We're going to be able to offer free current accounts without subsidy from other products.

"That's because we don't have the big network overheads of branches and legacy systems."

If she is right, then perhaps the greatest potential for a more modern form of banking may lie in emerging markets, where old-style infrastructure was never built.

Across Africa, for example, payment services using basic mobiles rather than smartphones are already widely available. In such markets, tech-savvy start-up lenders could do very well indeed.

Many believe, however, that the current phase of traditional financial services being challenged by upstart technology companies will morph into a new world order, in which the best of the old operators integrate the latest technology, either under their own steam or via acquisition.

"The financial services industry is at a crossroads," says Mr Ramakrishnan. "But we firmly believe that some firms will retake the initiative and challenge the new digital challengers at their own game.

"Indeed, we may see symbiotic relationships emerge, where both established firms and challengers come together to create a mutually beneficial partnership."

Marketers May Be Falling Out of Love With Product Placement; Product placement on broadcast TV has been on the decline for several years.

By Suzanne Vranica 
647 words
13 October 2015
22:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Ad clutter is generally increasing as marketers find more and more places to peddle their products. But there is one place where ads are declining: smack in the middle of prime time TV shows.

The number of product placements appearing during the first week of the new broadcast season declined 45% to 104, compared to last year's premiere week, according to Nielsen.

The number of in-program placements has declined for several seasons. There were 4,455 product placements made during last year's broadcast TV season, a 3% drop from the year-earlier season and down 20% from the 2012-2013 season.

Why the drop? Some blame the emergence of new advertising platforms.

 There are so many more options available for advertising now and every year its grows," said Chad Dreas, Nielsen's managing director of media analytics, pointing to the growth of digital advertising and social media advertising.

Cost is also keeping advertisers at bay. Integrations are a  big commitment," said Andy Donchin, chief investment officer at Amplify US, an ad-buying unit of Dentsu.  There is an out of pocket premium" that advertisers have to pay that sometimes can be hard to justify, he added.

Still, some of the world's biggest brands routinely use the ad tactic. Apple, Starbucks, Chevrolet, Ford and Google were the largest users of product placement last year on broadcast TV. Combined, those brands were responsible for 30% of all in-program placements during the last broadcast season, Nielsen said.

Product placement has been a part of the TV business since the early days of the medium. About a decade ago, the practice exploded as marketers saw it as a way to sidestep the damaging effects of TV commercial-skipping devices. At the same time, TV networks jumped on the opportunity and now they aggressively package product plugs into many of their ad deals.

That took the product placement business, which had been dominated by small firms that had relationships with set designers and Hollywood show runners, to a whole new level. Product plugs became more pronounced and elaborate. Advertisers were no longer satisfied with having a character on a show reach for a branded soda can   many sought bigger brand integrations that included getting the product or its attributes mentioned on the show. Indeed, there were roughly 8,500 in-program placements during the 2009-2010 broadcast season, Nielsen said.

Despite the recent decline in plugs, there is evidence to suggest that many advertisers still believe product placements works. During Stephen Colbert's debut as the host of CBS's  Late Show" last month, Mr. Colbert spent several minutes poking fun at the fact that he was obligated to promote Sabra roasted red pepper humus on the air.

Next week viewers will see messages from health insurer Cigna Corp. baked into some programs airing on Walt Disney's ABC. In an effort to promote how important getting a check-up from your doctor is, the insurance company worked with ABC to have some cast members of  Jimmy Kimmel Live" swap jobs, on air, so they can go to the doctor for a checkup.

Cigna said having its pitch embedded or mentioned in a program helps a TV viewer to better remember the message. The product integration  amplifies the commercial message," said Stephen Cassell, global branding officer at Cigna.

A recent Nielsen study found that when a product plug aired in the same program as standard commercials for the same brand, the ability for a consumer ages 18 to 49 to remember the brand increased 18%. Product integrations  add value and increase the effectiveness" of ads, added Mr. Dreas at Nielsen.

Write to Suzanne Vranica at "mailto:suzanne.vranica@wsj.com"
