In Depth: Company Tracks Bank Cards And Sells Data to Investors

By Bradley Hope 
2,110 words
10 August 2015
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
10
English
(Copyright (c) 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Yodlee Inc.'s main business is providing online personal-finance tools to 11 of the 20 largest U.S. banks in assets.

But when bank customers use those tools, Yodlee has another way of making money: The company sells some of the data it gathers from credit- and debit-card transactions to investors and research firms, which mine the information for clues about trends that can move stock prices.

"Yodlee can tell you down to the day how much the water bill was across 25,000 citizens of San Francisco" or the daily spending at McDonald's throughout the country, says Peter Hazlehurst, Yodlee's former chief product officer.

The details are so valuable that some investment firms have paid more than $2 million apiece for an annual subscription to Yodlee's service, according to people familiar with the matter. Buyers include Point72 Asset Management LLP and Tiger Global Management LLC.

Yodlee's side business marks an escalation in the long-running arms race among investors who try to turn data into investing profits. Card transactions are an especially coveted example of "alternative data," a broad term for information that goes beyond traditional measurements like stock-price history.

Some of the world's most sophisticated investors analyze location data from mobile-phone carriers, cargo-ship movements and construction permits scanned in from local-government offices across the U.S. The data troves can cost anywhere from $50,000 to $4 million a year, says Gene Ekster, who has held alternative-data-related jobs since 2008.

"It used to be that you'd hire people to go out and do research or count the number of people going into a store," Mr. Ekster says. "Now there is enormous effort in analyzing data and finding tradable signals from a range of new sources. It's still in the early stages."

The information being sold and sifted is more granular than ever.

Investment firms have used Yodlee's data to compare purchases made by visitors to specific theme parks owned by SeaWorld Entertainment Inc., including SeaWorld and Busch Gardens, say people familiar with the situation.

Investors can zoom in on transactions related to tickets, merchandise, food and parking. SeaWorld's securities filings and earnings announcements disclose only the company's overall revenue from admissions and "food, merchandise and other" sales. SeaWorld declines to comment.

Yodlee, based in Redwood City, Calif., hasn't publicly disclosed that it sells the data to investment firms. Before its initial public offering last year, Yodlee said it provides data for "multiple research uses," such as "to enhance predictive analysis."

The company won't identify its data-buying clients. The business is part of a larger operation that produced about 10% of Yodlee's total revenue last year, according to the company.

In a statement, Yodlee (pronounced YOHD-lee) says it has "a very limited number of strategic partnerships with firms to develop more sophisticated analytic solutions."

Yodlee also says it "adheres to strict privacy standards to ensure that the transaction data in our data products is anonymized and does not contain personally identifiable or attributable information," according to a statement.

The company says banks largely remove details that could be traced back to a specific person before the data is shipped to Yodlee. Yodlee then puts the data through a separate process that scrubs out any remaining identifiers.

Yodlee says its "strategic partners" are barred from "reselling, sharing or otherwise providing the de-identified transaction elements to any party" and are "further expressly prohibited from even attempting to re-identify any individual or any Yodlee customers from the data."

Some researchers have claimed they were able to "re-identify" people in anonymized data unrelated to Yodlee through the use of other data sets.

In January, researchers from the Massachusetts Institute of Technology said in Science magazine that they could unmask roughly 90% of people in a database of anonymous credit-card transactions with four pieces of information that included date and transaction location. They used a private database provided to them by an unidentified company.

Someone trying to identify a hypothetical person named Scott from a database of anonymized card transactions would need to know only a few pieces of additional information from other sources, the researchers said.

If Scott made a purchase at a certain bakery one day and ate dinner at a specific restaurant the next day, the person probably could "re-identify" Scott from the database, according to the researchers.

Yodlee says no one can be re-identified from its data set. "There is no possibility that anyone's identity can be determined," the company says in a statement.

Peter Swire, a privacy expert at Georgia Institute of Technology hired by Yodlee to review its privacy safeguards after questions from The Wall Street Journal, says the company is "doing the technical and administrative things that regulators have recommended" to reduce the risk of data being re-identified.

He says investment firms have no reason to reverse-engineer the information they buy from Yodlee because "they are not in the business of playing spy and figuring out what the transaction history of one individual or finding the name of an individual."

Investment firms say they take precautions with alternative data. "The world of data is a bit like the Wild West, and we want to and have to be on the highest of high roads with the data we process," says Mark Herr, a spokesman for Point72, one of Yodlee's biggest data buyers.

The Stamford, Conn., firm has $11 billion in assets and was formerly known as SAC Capital Advisors LP. In 2013, SAC agreed to plead guilty to insider-trading charges, pay $1.8 billion in fines and stop managing money for outside clients.

Point72 has a division called Aperio to find and analyze data on behalf of trading groups throughout the firm. Aperio attempts to glean insights from the "torrent of publicly available data out there," says a person familiar with Point72.

The spokesman adds that Point72 uses a compliance system to make sure the information doesn't contain any material, nonpublic or personally identifiable information.

Last year, Yodlee had revenue of $89.1 million, up 27% from $70.2 million in 2013. The company's annual revenue has nearly doubled since 2010 and is expected to climb as high as $110.5 million this year, Yodlee said in May.

Yodlee begins collecting transaction data from bank customers after they sign up to use online tools powered by Yodlee.

Some information also goes to Yodlee through a practice called "screen scraping." If a person uses a budgeting app created by Yodlee and gives the app permission to access their bank accounts using their username and password, Yodlee can capture the transaction history in those accounts.

Yodlee's clients access current and historic data through an online portal. Data available to some customers included billions of transactions since 2011 and was gathered from as many as six million "users," according to a document reviewed by The Wall Street Journal.

The document said individual transactions can include any of several pieces of information, such as transaction date, location, amount and merchant name. Yodlee says that location and merchant information "occurs only in a very small percentage of the transactions reported."

Some transactions can include numbers assigned by Yodlee to individual debit- and credit-cards. That helps investors and research firms that buy Yodlee's data track spending trends, say people familiar with the use of the data.

Mr. Swire, the privacy expert hired by Yodlee, says the identification numbers sometimes can change over time.

Banks have expressed few qualms about Yodlee, saying their contracts with the company specify what it is allowed to do with customer information. Credit- and debit-card users typically grant permission to the banks when they set up their account and agree to the bank's privacy policy.

HSBC Holdings PLC lets Yodlee use only "aggregated data that is combined with statistical information from other sources," according to an HSBC spokesman. Yodlee may not sell "individual client or HSBC-specific data."

A Citigroup Inc. spokesman says the data Yodlee has access to "contains no personally identifiable information." Bank of America Corp., which generated nearly 14% of Yodlee's revenue last year, says the bank "ensures all third-party vendors protect customer data at standards that meet our policies, regulatory guidance and requirements, and are in accordance with our customer privacy policy."

USAA, which focuses on serving current and former military members and their families, doesn't permit Yodlee to "store personally identifiable information about our members," according to a USAA spokeswoman.

Personal Capital Corp.'s app uses Yodlee's system to aggregate accounts so customers can see their overall net worth, cash flow, asset allocation and portfolio performance.

In response to questions about Yodlee, Saikat Maiti, Personal Capital's chief information security and trust officer, says in a statement that the firm is partners with a "third party that in the course of providing the service collects and anonymizes data that they may utilize."

After being contacted by the Journal, Personal Capital also added a new paragraph to its customer privacy disclosure that says outside firms might "be contractually allowed to rent, sell or otherwise make commercial use" of customer data.

Among Yodlee's largest customers, J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. doesn't participate in the data-selling side business. A spokeswoman for J.P. Morgan says the bank doesn't give Yodlee any access to transaction-level data and doesn't allow the company to sell such data.

Yodlee was started in 1999 and focused at first on building relationships with financial institutions. The company later developed a new business helping financial-app companies help customers track their finances.

Yodlee says more than 800 organizations were using "the Yodlee platform to power their consumer-facing digital offerings" as of Dec. 31.

Banks and other financial institutions that are Yodlee customers have more than 100 million customers. Banks pay Yodlee a fee every time a customer uses a tool powered by the company.

Yodlee's largest investors as of March 31 include mutual-fund giant Fidelity Investments, private-equity firm Warburg Pincus LLC and customers Bank of America and Tiger Global. Tiger Global, with $20 billion in assets, uses Yodlee data as part of its investment research.

In May, Yodlee Chairman, President and Chief Executive Anil Arora told analysts and investors that the company sees itself as the "leading cloud-based financial platform."

Yodlee has a "vision to transform digital financial services by simplifying and improving the lives of anyone with a financial account," he added. Mr. Arora cited as a growth opportunity "leveraging our unique big data assets and analytics capabilities."

Transaction data is especially useful for understanding trends at publicly traded restaurant companies, clothing firms and theme-park operators like SeaWorld, according to people familiar with the matter. Share prices of such companies are tied closely to quarterly revenue, so surprises can lead to big price swings.

Hedge-fund executives and people familiar with Yodlee say its data product is one of best and priciest available to investors, reflecting a growing strategy by information gatherers to cash in on "exhaust," or data collected while doing other business.

Yodlee's data customers include the investment research arm of Investment Technology Group Inc., say people familiar with the matter.

On its website, the New York brokerage says it has access to a "daily extended consumer transaction panel of over 3 million users and anonymized transaction level data from over 4 million accounts." ITG declines to comment on the sources of its data.

Data-analysis firm Verisk Analytics Inc., of Jersey City, N.J., a Yodlee rival, says it doesn't sell "customer-level spending data" but analyzes it for banks, retailers and other clients. Cardlytics Inc., based in Atlanta, doesn't "supply intelligence to any Wall Street firms," the company says in a statement.

At Yodlee, the data-crunching work is in such hot demand that the company has subcontracted the job of making sense of the information for some clients. The Earnest Research Co., a New York startup, analyzes data from Yodlee on behalf of certain hedge funds, say people familiar with the matter.

Earnest produces weekly reports on sales and transactions at more than 70 retail companies from Urban Outfitters Inc. to McDonald's Corp., these people said. Yodlee declines to comment on its relationship with Earnest, and Earnest didn't respond to requests for comment.

NATIONAL NEWS
Bean seeks statistics agency rethink as disquiet over its performance mounts

By Emily Cadman 
989 words
5 August 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
03
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Former bank deputy governor calls for evidence towards his evaluation of controversy-hit ONS

In the 1970s, says Sir Charlie Bean, the UK was "probably the world leader in statistics". As he prepares to tackle the question of whether the facts and figures produced by the Office for National Statistics are fit for purpose, Sir Charlie admits the UK is not quite "the leader of the pack" any more.

The Bank of England's former deputy governor was appointed last month by Chancellor George Osborne to lead an in-depth review of UK economic data.

Speaking to the Financial Times as he calls on the statistics community to contribute evidence, he stresses the ONS is "clearly still producing top quality statistics". Yet in government circles, disquiet with its performance has been growing.

Over the past few years, the ONS has been embroiled in a series of controversies. Its accounting treatment of the proceeds of quantitative easing, which it later reversed, was criticised for artificially flattering the public finances. Errors in a range of statistics such as construction and trade led to the stripping of the national statistics "kitemark" by the UK Statistics Authority.

It has endured scathing criticism of its website and creaking IT infrastructure, sparking concern that outside data terminals used by the likes of traders could publish the information before it was up on the official ONS site. That led to the controversial suggestion that crucial market-moving statistics might have to be released gradually to reduce pressure on its computer system.

One topic on the table is the issue of whether the ONS needs to re-establish a senior team in London.

When the ONS relocated to Newport in 2007 it lost nearly 90 per cent of its senior staff, and the team running core economic statistics suffered in particular. Many of those who left took up positions in the Treasury, the BoE or the City - which partly explains the chorus of criticism from those institutions.

Economists say one of the biggest issues since the move has been that the relationship between staff at the ONS, BoE and Treasury has been weakened because secondments between those organisations are harder if one of them is four hours away. That means ONS statisticians can no longer routinely puzzle over oddities in the data with colleagues in nearby institutions and res-olve them before mistakes are made. Jonathan Athow, the new head of economic statistics at the ONS, has said a small London team "could be helpful".

The other oft-repeated grumble is that the organisation has focused too heavily on the technicalities of data production and has lost track of what professional users actually need - how the statistics are organised and communicated. David Caplan, a former senior ONS staff member and now director of research and statistics at policy analysis firm Belmana, says: "Measuring the economy is tough, but the ONS needs to shift its attitude and understand and address users' concerns."

Sir Charlie says, "Ultimately it should be the needs of users, broadly interpreted, that determine what sorts of statistics should be produced" - leaving the door open for a rethink of what the agency's output should be.

Another large part of the review will cover whether the ONS is making use of all the possibilities of the big data revolution, as well as figures from other government sources. Governance arrangements for economic statistics will also be examined, but Sir Charlie says he regards "the independence of the provision of statistics as a total red line. You only have to look at countries like Greece to realise what can happen if that line gets crossed."

He has established a small team, with officials seconded from the BoE, Treasury and the UK Statistics Authority, to help him conduct the review. The timescale is tight: Sir Charlie will publish his interim report in the autumn and the final report is due before next year's Budget, meaning the chancellor could choose to look again at resourcing for the agency, which has seen cuts of about 20 per cent over the past five years, if the review recommends more money is needed. "They have sent me quite a tough exam with not that much time to do it," he jokes.

Background

From reviews and cuts to web difficulties and errors

1981 Rayner review leads to 25 per cent cut to the Government Statistical Service.

1988 Nigel Lawson sets up the Pickford review, after concerns the strength of the boom had been mismeasured.

1996 Office for National Statistics formed, merging statistical services

2005 Gordon Brown makes ONS independent of government .

2006 After the Lyons review, the ONS moves to Newport, Wales. The next year the BoE warns MPs the relocation "poses a serious risk to the maintenance of the quality of macroeconomic data".

2009 ONS admits it has overstated by 56 per cent the quantity of high street sales since the financial crisis.

2010 Government's statistics budget cut by 17.4 per cent in real terms over next five years. ONS postpones publication of all data on the national accounts for two weeks after it found "potential errors".

2011 New ONS website launched, to an outcry over difficulties in using it.

2013 ONS retains the RPI inflation index , despite accepting its upward bias. Later, the UK Statistics Authority raps ONS for treatment of cash flows from quantitative easing, which flatter net borrowing figures.

2014 John Pullinger, the new national statistician, puts statistics at the top of his agenda for reform at the ONS .

2015 In May, the UKSA warns that UK official trade data will remain vulnerable to errors until the ONS improves systems. In June, errors measuring inflation in the construction sector lead to an upward revision of annual growth in national income in the first quarter. Source: FT research, Royal Statistical Society

FRONT PAGE - FIRST SECTION
Shake-up aims to bring official statistics into internet era;  "Ex-BoE deputy Bean begins review  "Number crunchers put on notice

By Emily Cadman 
491 words
5 August 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
01
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Sir Charlie Bean, former deputy governor of the Bank of England, will today put the nation's number crunchers on notice that the status quo is untenable, arguing that the internet revolution has rendered Britain's official statistics out of date.

Speaking ahead of the launch of his official review into the state of the nation's economic numbers, he told the Financial Times the current framework for the national accounts "was developed in the aftermath of the Great Depression". While big data, rapid innovations in technology and the rise of the sharing economy had transformed industries around the globe, official -statistics had soldiered along largely unchanged, he said.

"As an economy develops, the traditional ways of thinking about it cease to be so relevant," he said, flagging concerns that official numbers may be incomplete in the internet age, where consumers, for example, book holidays online, bypassing travel companies.

In the 1970s the UK was "probably the world leader in statistics", he said, but it was not "quite the leader of the pack now". He queried whether the UK was making best use of opportunities stemming from the "big data" revolution.

After years of grumbling from policymakers and economists about the quality of the national statistics, the BoE's former chief economist was appointed last month by George Osborne, the chancellor, to lead a root and branch review of the UK's economic statistics.

Sir Charlie believes that the traditional divisions of industrial sectors are increasingly out of date. Citing Rolls-Royce, which the public considers an exemplar of manufacturing excellence, but in fact makes much of its money from its services business, he said talking about manufacturing and services as distinct concepts is "often not a helpful way to think about economic activity".

Central to the review will be the question of whether the Office for National Statistics, which has had a rocky period amid private and public criticism about the quality of its data, can do what policymakers want it to do. Jonathan Athow, the new head of economic statistics at the ONS, said it was "a good opportunity to look to the future and ask ourselves some searching questions about what we should be doing".

Over the past few years, the ONS has been embroiled in a series of controversies - from its accounting treatment of the proceeds of quantitative easing to errors in a range of statistics such as construction and trade.

Sir Charlie said the ONS had been on a "rollercoaster" in the past couple of -decades as it had seen many shake-ups, including its move in 2007 to Newport when it lost most of its experienced staff.

Simon Briscoe, an expert on official statistics, said the review had the "potential to be supremely important".

The dollar rose 0.4% versus the yen to 124.86 in late-afternoon trade, on track for its highest in two months. The Wall Street Journal Dollar Index, which compares the greenback against a basket of widely traded currencies, increased 0.1% to 89.14, its highest level in about five months and just 0.2% from a 12-year high it hit on March 13.

Investors moved into the dollar Wednesday after the Institute for Supply Management reported that "http://www.wsj.com/articles/ism-nonmanufacturing-survey-rises-to-60-3-in-july-1438784012" rose to 60.3 in July from 56.0 in June. The number soared above economists' forecast of 56.1. A reading above 50 signifies an expansion in activity.

In addition, the ISM employment index rose to 59.6 from 52.7, helping to offset a report on Wednesday by Automatic Data Processing and forecasting firm Moody'sAnalytics showing that private payrolls in the U.S. increased by "http://www.wsj.com/articles/private-sector-added-185-000-jobs-in-july-fewer-than-expected-1438778175".

Investors have been driving the dollar higher over much of the past year on wagers that the Fed will raise short-term interest rates for the first time since 2006 as the U.S. economy improves, a view supported by strong labor-market gains. Higher borrowing costs would boost the dollar's allure to investors searching for yield.

But the dollar's advance has mostly been halting since March, as weaker data early in 2015 raised doubts about the timing of the central bank's move, pushing back market expectations toward the latter half of 2015 from midyear, and even into 2016.

The market is looking to nonfarm payrolls numbers for July, which are released Friday morning, to provide more clarity on the state of the labor market and spark a renewed rally in the U.S. currency. Fed officials have recently communicated that the bar is low for a September rate increase, but a move still depends on a strong jobs reports underpinning investor conviction that U.S. labor market gains are holding.

 The scope for dollar gains might be limited ahead of the payrolls number," said Omer Esiner, chief market analyst at the currency brokerage Commonwealth Foreign Exchange, Inc.  A decent number on Friday, say 225,000 and above, keeps September on the table and paves the way for a more meaningful and sustained rally in the dollar."

Economists predict that the U.S. added 215,000 jobs in July, a number consistent with strong job growth. The U.S. has been averaging 233,000 monthly job gains over the past two years.

Dollar bulls got a lift on Tuesday after Federal Reserve Bank of Atlanta President Dennis Lockhart told The Wall Street Journal that only a  significant deterioration in the economic picture" "http://www.wsj.com/articles/atlanta-feds-lockhart-fed-is-close-to-being-ready-to-raise-short-term-rates-1438709252".

 Most investors won't make up their minds, and the Fed won't make up its mind, until they see the nonfarm payrolls data," said Daniel Katzive, head of currency strategy for North America at BNP Paribas.  And most price action this week was marking time ahead of Friday's release of those numbers."

In other trade, the dollar slid 0.2% against the euro, as the common currency bought $1.0904.

In Depth: Q&A: Citadel's Ken Griffin in Conversation

By Rob Copeland

The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
13
English
(Copyright (c) 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Ken Griffin is back the near the top of the hedge-fund world. The billionaire Mr. Griffin has not just rebuilt Citadel from its crisis-era lows, but pushed it to aggressively expand into new areas -- all while he contends with a high-profile divorce that has Chicago's gossip pages hanging on every filing. As he prepares to mark Citadel's 25th anniversary later this year, Mr. Griffin sat for several interviews with the Journal's Rob Copeland to discuss his investing style, the markets today and politics. Condensed excerpts follow:

Q: Your divorce has made public some unflattering allegations about you, many of which you've denied. How do you deal with the scrutiny?

A: Long ago I had to sort of learn to have a thick skin to read some of the things you read in the papers and to also keep my ego in check when you read some really flattering things in the papers. Two-thousand eight was a failure that I had to endure. I didn't foresee the collapse of the U.S. financial system -- I just didn't see that coming -- that the U.S. banking sector would survive only because of the support and lifeline from the U.S. government. I then had to fight it out in the 3 years and 17 days post-08 to make back the losses that we incurred in 16 weeks. So that's a backdrop.

In the last year, it's regrettable that the strategy used by the now-withdrawn opposing counsel was to try to attack my character in my hometown. That didn't work. My actions speak louder than anything else. Let me be clear: All I really care about is my kids know that their parents love them.

Q: Tell me something about yourself that people might find surprising.

A: It drives people nuts that I take them to McDonald's all the time. It's the call from Ken: 'Let's go for a walk.' Everyone knows that is code for, 'We're going to McDonald's.' People have these images of what the successful business person is supposed to act like and be like and so forth.

Q: What is it like to see your personal life, business life and political giving become headline news?

A: For choice, I prefer not to be a public figure. I don't have Tom Cruise's good looks. I don't have a need to be on the front page of fill-in-the-blank. On my political giving, look, there's probably not a week that goes by that I don't realize the sacrifices that our forefathers made for this country. Yes, I'm involved in politics. Because frankly, if not me, who?

Q: You've given millions to back Republican Bruce Rauner for governor of Illinois, yet also boosted Democrat Rahm Emanuel for mayor of Chicago. Where do your politics lie?

A: I can't describe myself as a die-hard Republication or faithful Democrat. The distinction between our two parties in the United States is pretty narrow. We need a mayor who understands how to get through a balance sheet; it was an easy call to get behind Rahm. I want Bruce to be comfortable knowing that he's got my support however he feels it's best to use. Anyone who knows him knows you don't have influence with Bruce.

Q: Where are you leaning in the 2016 presidential race?

A: I love Walker for taking on the entrenched interests in Wisconsin. I want to see how he can do on the national stage. I think Rubio's got a really compelling message and he comes from a background that gives him real insight into the challenges that the middle class faces. I wish Jeb had any last name but Bush, because I think Jeb's track record is really good. As an American, I'm uncomfortable with the idea of family political dynasties. We don't need another Clinton and we don't need another Bush.

Q: How is Citadel dealing with the recent market volatility in China (note: interview conducted before one of the firm's accounts in China was suspended by regulators)

A: When I think about the next 10 years, we are going to have a very meaningful business in China. We've seen the government of China respond very vigorously to the issues at hand. This is the first time they've seen so much wealth disappear so quickly in such a visible way. I think they over-responded but you can understand why they did so. They just haven't experienced this before culturally as a regime. This is a very interesting moment for them. The 10-year story on China is completely intact.

Q: How is the firm doing things differently now than it did five or 10 years ago?

A: We recently hired someone with a machine learning background from Amazon.com, and one of the problems he worked on there was the routing of calls from its call center. What's the applicability of that? Amazon, as you can imagine, has endless calls every day from its customers and if they can route those calls more quickly and more efficiently the customer experience is better. The actual mathematical techniques that Amazon uses to think about that problem we think have applicability to the financial markets. It's neat.

Q: Many of your competitors are shying from calling themselves "hedge funds." Do you have ambitions for Citadel to be something else?

A: Over the years, there have been moments in time where the word "hedge fund" in the popular media has had positive connotations and negative connotations, but the word has a very strong historical anchoring to me. We're here to create wealth for our stakeholders in good times. We're here hopefully to create wealth in difficult times.

(See related article: "Citadel Is Back and in No Mood to Be Cautious" -- WSJE August 5, 2015)

Spy Software Gets A Job on Wall Street

By Bradley Hope 
1,180 words
4 August 2015
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
1
English
(Copyright (c) 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

FRANKLIN, Tenn. -- Spies are infiltrating Wall Street.

A wave of companies with ties to the intelligence community is winning over the world of finance, with banks and hedge funds putting the firms' terrorist-tracking tools to work rooting out employee misconduct before it leads to fines or worse.

"Both Wall Street and the intelligence world want the same thing: to find unknown unknowns in the data," said Roger Hockenberry, the former chief technology officer of the Central Intelligence Agency's clandestine services and now a partner at the consulting firm Cognitio Corp. in Washington.

"Financial firms aren't looking for terrorists, but good customers and attempts at fraud," he said.

The CIA gave many of these companies their big break: After the terror attacks of September 2001, a private-equity arm of the CIA known as In-Q-Tel began seeding companies that could help it sift through vast repositories of data to quickly identify threats. Those skills have become more valuable on Wall Street as firms try to keep up with rogue traders in increasingly complex and rapidly moving markets.

Of 101 companies publicly seeded by In-Q-Tel , 33 have taken on Wall Street clients in recent years, according to a review by The Wall Street Journal. A spokeswoman for In-Q-Tel declined to comment.

Other companies in this field include Palantir Technologies Inc ., which helps government and Wall Street clients analyze their data; Recorded Future Inc ., which monitors the Internet for coalescing cybersecurity threats and other events; and Domino Data Labs Inc., which provides the plumbing for firms to do data analytics.

Palantir raised $450 million last week at a valuation of about $20 billion. Since 2009, it has received more than $215 million in contracts with the Federal Bureau of Investigation , Defense Department and Department of Homeland Security , according to USAspending.gov, a federal site that publishes government contracts. The company, which got its start working for intelligence agencies, now says more than 60% of its business comes from commercial clients, particularly on Wall Street.

Another firm, a small technology company in Tennessee called Digital Reasoning, has counted Swiss bank UBS Group AG , New York hedge-fund firm Point72 Asset Management LLP and Credit Suisse Group AG as customers of data-combing software the U.S. government has been using to track down enemies of the state.

Tim Estes founded the firm that became Digital Reasoning in 2000 during his last year of college at the University of Virginia , where he studied philosophy.

Conceived as a company specializing in "machine learning," a branch of computer science dealing with programs that can learn from data and make better predictions, Digital Reasoning struggled during its first couple of years.

But one day in 2002, Mr. Estes landed a meeting at a nearby Army base. He demonstrated an early version of the software product that came to be known as Synthesys, showing how the company's systems could read vast amounts of text and extract patterns and links not apparent to the human eye.

That led to a pilot project and an initial government contract in 2004. The system became embedded in Army systems and was used in the field in Afghanistan, according to Digital Reasoning.

An In-Q-Tel investment in 2011 precipitated an even bigger mandate for Digital Reasoning from a wider swath of agencies, including the CIA.

Located in a glassy office block on the outskirts of Franklin, Tenn., the firm's staff of about 100 includes more than 30 employees with top-secret security clearance. Former Wall Street researchers and young technologists shuffle in and out of conference rooms named after locations from the "Lord of the Rings" trilogy.

"We see ourselves as the Darwinian outcome of billions of dollars of intelligence spending post-9/11," said Mr. Estes, 36 years old.

Digital Reasoning's government work is classified but Mr. Estes said it involves things like "figuring out the aliases of certain people that were very hard to find in the data."

Last year, Digital Reasoning raised $24 million from a group of banks led by Goldman Sachs Group Inc . and Credit Suisse . So far, it has publicized contracts to assist the compliance teams of UBS and Point72, the $11 billion firm run by Steven A. Cohen that was formerly known as SAC Capital .

Point72 uses systems from Digital Reasoning in conjunction with those from Palantir to monitor roughly one million emails, instant messages and other electronic communications every week, according to Vincent Tortorella, the firm's chief compliance and surveillance officer.

Systems from the two companies help "read, flag and risk-score emails," which are then reviewed by a compliance group composed of former employees of the CIA, FBI , Department of Homeland Security and the Securities and Exchange Commission , he said.

Mr. Cohen's firm has been caught in regulators' cross hairs in recent years for issues related to its oversight of employees. In 2013, SAC agreed to plead guilty to insider-trading charges, pay $1.8 billion in fines and stop managing money for outside clients. Mr. Cohen is still awaiting the outcome of a civil case for failure to supervise employees later found guilty of insider trading.

UBS , Credit Suisse and Goldman Sachs declined to comment.

In pitching prospective clients, Digital Reasoning often shows a demonstration of how its system responded when it was fed 500,000 emails related to the Enron scandal made available by the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission .

After being "taught" some key concepts about compliance, the Synthesys program identified dozens of suspicious emails in which participants were using language that suggested attempts to conceal or destroy information. In some instances the program detected a lack of "business confidence," which in the Enron case was primarily related to accounting practices.

The Synthesys system reads all the text files in a database, capturing metadata and creating links between people and institutions. Words are given sentiment scores to help detect emotion. The end result is an application that can be used to look at the data from many perspectives.

With training, the system can identify situations in which people are using code words for something nefarious, said Matthew Russell , the firm's chief technology officer and a former intelligence officer in the U.S. Air Force .

For example, an extremist group might refer to bombs as wedding cakes and bombings as weddings. In finance, the system might look for "gift-giving" language, such as a discussion of seats at a sporting event or other event. The software might also monitor for attempts to conceal information, phrases that seem to be pressuring someone to do something or expressions of anger. Some institutions search for words or phrases that show a lack of confidence around a decision, which could suggest they are wading into gray area.

Digital Reasoning is already in discussions with financial clients about the next phase of analysis after compliance solutions: increasing revenue.

---

Elizabeth Dwoskin contributed to this article.

China's Growth Targets Will Be Met

By Mark A. DeWeaver 
831 words
3 August 2015
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
14
English
(Copyright (c) 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

China's stock-market crash has raised fears of global contagion not witnessed since the 2008 financial crisis. Such fears are misplaced. Dropping share prices, even to the extent seen last week, doesn't mean Beijing won't hit its 7% GDP growth target or will abruptly cease implementing its ambitious monetary and fiscal stimulus programs.

On June 27, the People's Bank of China announced a 25 basis-point interest-rate cut -- the fourth such cut since November. But lowering interest rates is a relatively ineffective way to stimulate the Chinese economy. China's state sector, which still accounts for a large share of economic activity, isn't sufficiently interest-rate sensitive for standard monetary-policy tools to work well.

Rather, it is Beijing's recent moves to ease fiscal policy that will ensure that this year's growth target can be met. Unlike traditional Keynesian stimulus programs, which are typically conducted at the central-government level, in China fiscal easing primarily involves providing additional state-bank money to local governments.

This has a more immediate and powerful effect on GDP growth and job creation, but it comes at a high cost: overinvestment in local projects and the misallocation of capital. China's landscape is littered with unused highways and airports, redundant steel and cement plants, unnecessary municipal office buildings and "ghost cities" filled with empty high-rises and deserted shopping malls.

From 2009 to 2013, "ineffective investment" amounted to a stunning 41.8 trillion yuan ($6.742 trillion), according to research published in 2014 by Xu Ce of China's National Development and Reform Commission and Wang Yuan of the Academy of Macroeconomic Research.

That China is heading down this path again can only mean that it has no other way to reach its growth target. It is also an indication of how little the economic system has changed despite the leadership's much-vaunted reform initiatives and efforts to tackle corruption at all levels of government.

Local governments have been under increased stress since the global financial crisis, when many of them borrowed large amounts to pay for a variety of fixed-asset investments. This spending helped China to keep growing even as year-over-year export growth plummeted to minus 25.7% in February 2009 from 19.2% in June 2008. It also left behind a mountain of debt that many localities have had trouble servicing. The Chinese Academy of Social Sciences recently put total year-end 2014 local government debt at 30.3 trillion yuan.

In March the Ministry of Finance announced a plan to alleviate the problem by allowing regional authorities to issue bonds directly for the first time. So far bonds worth 2 trillion yuan have been authorized -- the first trillion in May and a second trillion in June. As of July 15, 1.2 trillion yuan in bonds had already been issued. While not a large percentage of the total amount local governments owe, these offerings will greatly enhance their solvency by providing funds to repay loans due this year.

On May 11 the State Council, China's cabinet, also issued a notification requiring the banks to "actively support" existing local projects. At the same time, a number of ideas for new investment spending are now under consideration. A recent article in the China Securities Times identified 10 provinces that had proposed "economic stability" measures since the beginning of May. These will rely heavily on infrastructure investment, which, as of the first half of 2015, is already up 19.1% year on year.

In addition to infrastructure, the localities are considering investments related to Beijing's "Internet Plus" strategy, which envisages a transformation of the Chinese economy based on cloud computing, big data, mobile applications and the fast-growing connected-devices market known as the Internet of things.

The term Internet Plus was first introduced by Premier Li Keqiang in his report to the annual session of the National People's Congress in March. While there have been many official pronouncements on this subject since then, it is still unclear what the new policy will entail.

A State Council executive meeting on June 24 approved a wide range of priority areas, covering everything from logistics and smart grids to environmental protection and the modernization of agriculture. Officials appear to have been given a broad mandate to invest in practically anything that can be justified as Internet related.

This year local government projects are set to make a major contribution to keeping China's GDP growth on target. Once again, however, much of the new investment will be inefficient, contributing to the country's already considerable stock of underutilized infrastructure while perhaps introducing novel species of IT white elephants as well.

---

Mr. DeWeaver is manager of the emerging markets fund Quantrarian Asia Hedge and the author of "Animal Spirits With Chinese Characteristics" (Palgrave Macmillan, 2012).

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - MONEY
Investors take 2.7bn from M&G flagship fund; BOND ASSETS

By Judith Evans 
529 words
1 August 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
05
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Underperformance stems from cautious approach

Investors pulled 2.7bn out of M&G's flagship Optimal Income fund in three months after a decline in performance resulting from cautious positioning by its well-known manager Richard Woolnough.

The bond fund, which remains one of Europe's largest, shrank from 23.8bn in March to 20.9bn at the end of June, according to figures from Morningstar. In performance, it ranks 61st out of 79 in its sector over the past year, figures from FE Analytics show.

The underperformance was "primarily because of the short-duration positioning which held back returns at a time when European government bonds saw sharp yield compression," said Jason Hollands, managing director at the wealth manager Tilney Bestinvest.

Performance has recovered more recently, Mr Hollands added. Over the past three months, the fund ranks 34th in its category; over a five-year period, its performance remains top-quartile.

M&G said it would not comment on fund flows.

M&G Optimal Income , an unconstrained vehicle that can rove through global bond markets and can also buy equities, was the UK's best-selling retail fund last year despite a 3 per cent entry fee.

Investors were drawn to its flexibility and strong nine-year record amid uncertain bond markets, but performance suffered in the past year from a decision to shorten duration, or sensitivity to interest rates, in preparation for rate rises that have not yet materialised. This meant the fund did not take part in a government bond rally last year.

The big outflows were partly due to the fund's popularity with continental European retail investors, who tend to move money rapidly in response to performance, said Juliet Schooling Latter, research director at Chelsea Financial Services.

"M&G is suffering more than other strategic bond funds," she added. The M&G fund made up the lion's share of outflows from the strategic bond sector, according to the Investment Association, while investors also pulled 536m from corporate bond funds.

The product has, meanwhile, faced criticism over its ballooning size . Jake Moeller, head of UK and Ireland research at Lipper, said: "Those guys are excellent bond fund managers, but when you've got 1,500 securities in a bond fund, it's too many. You will approach an index position. It becomes much harder to outperform - that's true of any fund."

Mr Woolnough told an interviewer at Fidelity Personal Investing in May: "The larger a fund is, then by definition it makes it harder to add individual stock selection ideas. But it also depends what the purpose of the fund is.

"Investors should be aware that the larger a fund gets, the harder it gets to add value in individual stock selection, and that's something we've faced over the last 10 years."

Mr Woolnough has been identified as the highest-paid employee of Prudential, M&G's parent company, and company documents say he was paid more than 15m in each of the past two years.

WORLD NEWS
Eurozone close to deflation as energy prices remain low; Statistics agency

By Duncan Robinson in Brussels and James Politi in Rome 
471 words
1 August 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
05
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The eurozone remained on the edge of deflation in July after inflation across the bloc held steady at 0.2 per cent, according to a flash estimate from the EU's statistical agency.

Data from Eurostat show that falling energy prices helped nullify the inflationary effects of the European Central Bank's programme of quantitative easing launched this year.

Core inflation, which strips out volatile items such as energy, accelerated 0.2 percentage points to 1 per cent year on year as the lower euro pushed up the costs of some goods.

Falling energy prices , including the big drop in oil, held back inflation across the eurozone. Energy prices' slide accelerated, declining 5.6 per cent in July compared with a 5.1 per cent drop the previous month.

Elsewhere, inflation in services hit its highest level in a year at 1.2 per cent, with analysts at Barclays suggesting that "technical factors", such as package holidays and airfare bookings, were behind some of this increase.

The overall estimate of 0.2 per cent was in line with expectations, although some analysts had pencilled in a return to very modest deflation this month.

Others were bullish that inflation would creep up. Tomas Holinka, an economist at Moody'sAnalytics, said: "Inflation should gather momentum in coming months as oil deflation eases and food price growth gains ground."

Inflation has been sluggish even in fast-growing economies such as Spain , which this week recorded expansion of 1 per cent quarter on quarter. Consumer prices in the country dipped 0.1 per cent year on year during July.

Unemployment remained stable at 11.1 per cent across the eurozone in June, according to Eurostat. Big falls in unemployment in Spain and Portugal were drowned out by worsening figures in Italy, Belgium and Austria.

Italy's unemployment climbed to 12.7 per cent in June from 12.5 per cent in May, delivering a blow to hopes that the nascent recovery in the eurozone's third-largest economy could bring some relief to its labour market.

Meanwhile, youth joblessness in the country increased to 44.2 per cent, its highest level since 1977 and roughly twice the eurozone average.

The discouraging data could hurt the government of Matteo Renzi , which has been hoping that its aggressive economic reform agenda could start delivering a brighter economic outlook for most Italians. But, so far, only pockets of the Italian economy have benefited from the country's tepid rebound.

Finland endured an even worse performance, with unemployment jumping from 8.6 per cent to 9.5 per cent.

Across the EU as a whole, unemployment remained static at 9.6 per cent in June.

UPS Deal Aims To Fill Up Trucks

By Laura Stevens and Paul Page 
783 words
1 August 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright  2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

United Parcel Service Inc. has agreed to buy Coyote Logistics LLC for $1.8 billion, looking to expand in the burgeoning freight-brokerage business and get a better handle on the peak holiday shipping season.

UPS, which is acquiring the shipping-services provider from private-equity firm Warburg Pincus LLC, said Friday it expects the addition to start contributing to earnings next year, eventually pitching in $100 million to $150 million.

UPS has been contracting with Chicago-based Coyote since 2012 to find extra truck space for the growing flood of holiday e-commerce, UPS Chief Commercial Officer Alan Gershenhorn said in an interview. The delivery giant has been working on reducing costs after tough back-to-back holiday seasons damped profit.

A prized addition to UPS's portfolio will be Coyote's proprietary technology, Mr. Gershenhorn said. The company will use it to make better use of its fleet of long-haul trucks, some of which at times drive empty between warehouses or package-sorting hubs. Filling that space should boost efficiency and revenue. UPS will also offer its customers use of the technology to fill out their own trucks.

Founded in 2006, Coyote was an early entrant into a new world of freight that used the promise of combining technology and analytics to win the business of retailers and manufacturers.

Founder Jeff Silver said Coyote hires young employees with no experience in transportation and trains them on an electronic platform that connects brokers and customers. The software helps spot where shipping needs of customers overlap, allowing brokers to find space on trucks more easily and to offer cheaper prices in buying the capacity, Mr. Silver said.

"We become a network integrator for our customers," he said. "It gets us out of the role of just being a traditional broker."

Coyote's technology has helped it rise quickly in a competitive, yet highly fragmented, market. The company booked $2.1 billion in revenue last year, according to UPS. According to Armstrong & Associates, a research firm, Coyote ranks 17th by revenue among third-party logistics providers, a wider universe of companies that also includes freight brokers. UPS Supply Chain Solutions ranked fourth with $5.76 billion in revenue, though it had largely steered clear of freight brokering before Friday's deal.

In a separate matter, UPS rival FedEx Corp. hit a bump with its planned acquisition of TNT Express NV. The European Commission on Friday opened an in-depth investigation into Fedex's nearly $5 billion deal for the Dutch delivery company, saying it was concerned about the merged company's dominance in international delivery in some markets.

The regulator said it would take until early December to decide whether it will approve the deal or ask for concessions to ease its concerns. The two other large international delivery companies, UPS and DHL Worldwide Express Inc., may not provide sufficient competition to the merged company, the antitrust watchdog said.

On its earnings call in June, FedEx executives said they did "not believe that the transaction faces any competition issues for the Commission." A similar attempt to acquire TNT by UPS collapsed in 2013 when it failed to satisfy European regulators.

TNT Express said in a release that the extended review by the European Commission is customary. Analysts said the review wasn't surprising and had been largely expected by the market.

FedEx said it continues to make progress on regulatory steps, which would allow it to complete the transaction in the first half of 2016. "We will continue to work together with TNT Express to meet the European Commission's need for additional due diligence and are confident that the combination of both companies will increase competition and create benefits for customers," said Patrick Fitzgerald, senior vice president integrated marketing and communications at FedEx.

As delivery companies look for growth, third-party logistics providers like Coyote are becoming more attractive. Revenue from those U.S. logistics providers rose 7.4% to $157.2 billion last year, faster than the 2.8% growth in logistics spending overall, according to research firm Armstrong & Associates.

FedEx acquired logistics company GENCO Distribution System Inc., a company that specializes in product returns, for $1.4 billion earlier this year.

Europe has also become increasingly attractive for growth. UPS has said it is doubling its investment there to nearly $2 billion over five years, while XPO Logistics Inc. earlier this year said it was purchasing French contract-logistics firm Norbert Dentressangle SA in a deal valued at $3.53 billion.

Cohen's Point72 Goes Big on Data

By Bradley Hope 
535 words
31 July 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright  2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

The $11 billion investment firm run by Steven A. Cohen continued its push into the world of big data, hiring the former co-head of research at hedge-fund giant Bridgewater Associates LP to be its new chief market intelligence officer.

Matthew Granade, 38 years old, will oversee Point72 Asset Management LP's data sourcing and analysis efforts. He said in an interview that he decided to join the firm after Mr. Cohen and Point72's president, Douglas Haynes, extolled the firm's efforts in data and analytics.

"Steve explained that this was the way the firm needs to evolve," he said. "My personal feeling is that this is a very exciting time in this space. Investing now isn't just about earnings estimates and 10ks. It's about satellite imagery, sensors and mobile devices. The more you can process those things, the more edge you'll have."

Formerly known as SAC Capital Advisors LP, Point72 has been trying to distance itself from its past since SAC agreed in 2013 to plead guilty to insider-trading charges, pay $1.8 billion in fines and stop managing money for outside clients. The firm also lost much of its senior investment staff, and Mr. Cohen still is awaiting the outcome of a civil case from the Securities and Exchange Commission for failure to supervise employees later found guilty of insider trading.

Mr. Cohen long has been known as a stock picker who makes major decisions on his own, even while the firm invested in new analytical tools. It isn't clear how much these efforts have changed its investment approach.

Point72's dive into data comes at a time of growing interest among Wall Street firms into how to find profit-driving information from torrents of data.

So-called quantitative investment firms, such as $25 billion Two Sigma Investments, long have been big consumers of data. Such firms use mathematics to sift through huge amounts of data for clues about the direction of stock prices or futures contracts. They analyze everything from social-media sentiment to satellite imagery that shows them how many cars are in parking lots of big retailers.

Investment firms that focus on more-traditional measures of companies' health also have sought to increase their use of sophisticated data. Oil investors for years have bought expensive information from companies that fly helicopters over storage facilities with infrared cameras that help detect the volume of oil stored inside.

Mr. Granade said new types of data have been emerging with greater frequency.

For example, after he left Bridgewater Mr. Granade served as an adviser to Orbital Insights Inc., a firm in Palo Alto, Calif., that has designed systems to analyze satellite images for useful information. The company's first clients have been hedge funds.

Point72 has several data teams that develop insights for portfolio managers. Its Aperio group has about 30 people who find different sources of data, analyze it and then give it to trading groups.

Another group, called Canvas, is in charge of more-traditional Wall Street research and conducting surveys. Point72 also has several economists.

By John Aglionby

English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Sale of Elster meters business to Honeywell realises 3.3bn

Investors in Melrose Industries are set to receive more than 2bn after the company sold its Elster meter business to Honeywell International of the US for 3.3bn.

The cash sale marks the biggest deal yet for the UK company, which operates a private equity-style model. Its value is 30 per cent larger than Melrose's market capitalisation before the announcement.

Melrose bought Elster for 1.8bn in August 2012, including an infusion of 1.2bn from shareholders, and boosted its operating margins from 12.8 to almost 20 per cent.

Simon Peckham , chief executive, said the price represented "a good deal for both parties". "We've done lots of work to [Elster] but there's more to do," he said.

Melrose's shares jumped to 295p in early trading before falling back to 279p, up 24.7p and a 9.71 per cent gain on Monday's close.

Mr Peckham said Melrose would look for acquisition targets, particularly in North America and northern Europe. "The requirement is good quality industrial businesses that aren't quite fulfilling their potential," he said, without identifying anything specific.

The exact amount that shareholders will receive has yet to be fixed. Melrose plans to pay off some 750m in debt, plus fees and long-term incentives to Elster management. The group also intends to keep some of the purchase price to meet operating expenses.

Analysts at Investec, Melrose's broker, estimated shareholders might receive as much as 2.3bn.

If they do, Melrose board members, who own 3.65 per cent of the company, are set to take home more than 84m on completion, expected in the first quarter of 2016.

A person with knowledge of the deal said Honeywell had been "hovering for some time" but that the acquisition process was "fairly rapid".

Elster employs 6,800 people and operates in 39 countries, with its main operations in the US, Germany, the UK and Slovakia.

Dave Cote , Honeywell's chairman and chief executive, said the acquisition would "generate strong returns for Honeywell's shareholders because it increases our growth profile globally" and Elster's growth can be accelerated.

The sale price implies a valuation of 3.1 times 2014 revenue and 14.3 times 2014 headline earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortisation, according to Melrose.

Mr Cote said Honeywell was attracted to Elster because utility metering was "rapidly evolving as new 'smart' technologies and software and data analytics capabilities are being adopted around the world".

He added that Elster "creates a new platform for acquisition targets for Honeywell".

Melrose's sole remaining asset is Brush Turbogenerators, a manufacturer of generators for steam and gas turbine applications. Melrose cautioned yesterday that "current trading conditions remain challenging for Brush". But it expected a better second half.

Christopher Miller , Melrose's executive chairman, said that in the 10 years since its first investment the company had created more than 2bn of shareholder value. "We look forward to beating that performance over the next decade," he added.

Last year Melrose said it would return 600m to shareholders after it sold five units from its FKI business for 945m. It said then it had more than tripled shareholder value in five years of ownership.

In 2005, it bought McKechnie, along with Dynacast, for an enterprise value of 429m. In the 18 months of its ownership of McKechnie, Melrose estimated the increase in value at nearly 2.5 times.

Dynacast was sold in July 2011 for an enterprise value of 377m, of which 373m was returned to shareholders.

Rothschild was Melrose's lead financial adviser and joint sponsor with JPMorgan also acting for them.

Additional reporting by Nathalie Thomas

Lone Star Makes Big Bet on London With Quintain Estates Acquisition; $1 Billion buy is latest by North American investor in U.K. capital

By Ed Ballard and Art Patnaude

The Wall Street Journal Online

Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

LONDON Private-equity firm Lone Star Funds is making a big bet on London's residential market with the acquisition of Quintain Estates & Development PLC, a developer that owns the land around Wembley Stadium, for 700 million ($1.09 billion).

Lone Star on Wednesday said it would pay 131 pence a share for Quintain, representing a 22.4% premium on its closing price Tuesday, and prompting shares in the U.K.-listed business to soar to their highest level in more than seven years.

 The proposed acquisition represents a unique opportunity for Lone Star to gain further exposure to residential and commercial assets in London," said Angus Dodd, managing director at Lone Star Europe.

Mr. Dodd added that the Dallas-based firm would use its funds to speed up Quintain's building plans to take advantage of London's property boom.

Figures released by the U.K.'s Land Registry on Tuesday showed house price growth in the capital continues to outpace the rest of the U.K., with average growth of 1.8% in June compared with 1.1% across the country. Average prices gained 9.2% to 481,820 in the 12 months through June, compared with 5.4% across the U.K.

Quintain is developing an 85-acre site around the storied Wembley Stadium, which hosts the England soccer team, rock concerts, and regular National Football League fixtures.

In a bid to spruce up the formerly shabby neighborhood, Quintain built a retail complex before it started selling apartments, targeting buy-to-let investors and young professionals rather than the richest buyers. Much of the land remains undeveloped. Overall, Quintain, whose board is recommending Lone Star's offer, plans to build around 5,000 homes over the next 10 years.

Lone Star is the latest North American investor to push into the London property market, despite warnings from some analysts that the market looks overheated.

Of the $26.6 billion invested into the U.K. capital from overseas so far this year, around $11 billion has come from the U.S. and Canada, according to Real Capital Analytics, a data firm.

Earlier this year, Qatar's state investment fund and Canadian firm Brookfield Property Partners LP"http://www.wsj.com/articles/brookfield-qatar-fund-take-control-of-londons-canary-wharf-1425407733"in London's Canary Wharf business district, where construction of "http://www.wsj.com/articles/buyers-queue-up-for-canary-wharf-condos-1436866381" is under way.

For Lone Star, the Quintain deal is its second big move in U.K. real estate this month, having earlier said it would bundle 89 U.K. hotels it already owns into a new company, Amaris Hospitality, which it plans to float on the London Stock Exchange.

For Quintain, the deal follows its rehabilitation following the financial crisis, during which it had to issue equity to stay afloat as London's property values plunged. Since then, it has sold a string of assets, including a development project in Greenwich that was acquired by Hong Kong billionaire Henry Cheng Kar-shun, and concentrated its development activity on Wembley.

Analysts at broker Liberum Capital said Lone Star's offer price understates Quintain's long-term earnings potential, but predicted that shareholders would nevertheless seize the chance to cash in before London's property market changes course again.

Global Finance: SNL Financial Deal Is Part of Data Trend

By Timothy W. Martin 
500 words
28 July 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright  2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Big data is fetching a bigger price tag.

McGraw Hill Financial Inc. agreed to buy SNL Financial LC for $2.2 billion in a deal announced Monday, nearly five times what the financial-data provider was worth four years ago when private-equity firm New Mountain Capital LLC purchased a 60% stake.

New York-based McGraw Hill outmaneuvered a handful of media and financial-data companies in its pursuit of SNL, based in Charlottesville, Va., according to people familiar with the matter. The pursuers included Hearst Corp., which has a majority ownership stake in bond-rating firm Fitch Ratings, as well as financial-data provider FactSet Research Systems Inc., according to a person familiar with the matter. A Hearst spokesman declined to comment. A FactSet spokeswoman didn't respond to a request for comment.

Investors weren't pleased with the multiple McGraw Hill agreed to pay, driving shares down 5.7%, or $5.99, to $99.59.

Analyst Timothy McHugh of William Blair & Co. said the price for SNL "is probably a little high," in a Monday note to investors. Another, William Bird of FBR Capital Markets & Co., said "the stock reaction seems exaggerated." Mr. Bird estimated the multiple paid by McGraw Hill was more than 43 times SNL's 2015 earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of about $51 million.

McGraw Hill Chief Financial Officer Jack Callahan acknowledged SNL "was certainly not cheap" but said his company's sales staff outside of the U.S. could help broaden SNL's reach faster.

McGraw Hill's tie-up with SNL caps a string of pricey deals for data-driven companies as the market for firms selling obscure financial intelligence on banks, commodities and real estate attracts a bigger audience and entrenched firms look to expand.

Carlyle Group LP and other investors last year agreed to acquire Dealogic, a financial-data provider, for about $700 million. In March, a consortium led by Carlyle and Warburg Pincus LLC bought ratings firm DBRS Ltd. for more than $500 million based, in part, on the promise of its ability to sell financial intelligence to banks and other investors. More transactions are expected as other firms rush to meet the data demand.

Financial-data and transactions giants First Data Corp. and SunGard recently filed for initial public offerings, and SunGard has had suitors express interest in an acquisition, The Wall Street Journal has reported. Shares of TransUnion, the credit bureau, are up 10% since their IPO debut last month.

"There's an increasing demand for data, research and analytics in the financial and corporate markets," said Douglas Peterson, McGraw Hill's chief executive and a former Citigroup Inc. officer, in an interview.

Separately, McGraw Hill reported that second-quarter profit grew to $353 million, or $1.28 a share, from $292 million, or $1.06 a share, a year earlier.

So Long Wall Street, Hello Silicon Valley

By Ryan Dezember and Vipal Monga 
998 words
28 July 2015
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
21
English
(Copyright (c) 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

More top Wall Streeters are California dreaming.

A technology-fueled gold rush is drawing seasoned financial executives with the promise of sunshine, fresh managerial challenges and compensation that can top even the seven-figure paychecks common in the investment world.

Blackstone Group LP said Friday that its chief financial officer, Laurence Tosi, is leaving the private-equity firm to become finance chief at Airbnb Inc., the booming home-rental service. He is just the latest Wall Street executive to move west to take advantage of massive investor interest in fast-growing companies seeking to upend entire swaths of the economy.

Mr. Tosi, 47 years old, has made a good living by any standard since he joined Blackstone following its 2007 initial public offering. He earned about $15 million last year, according to filings, and $33.8 million over the past three years, not including dividends and some other perks, such as proceeds from investments made in the firm's funds.

Yet those sums will likely appear paltry next to his winnings should Airbnb pull off a successful IPO in coming years, as many analysts expect.

Airbnb, a seven-year-old San Francisco company, is working to complete private funding arrangements that value it at $25.5 billion. Mr. Tosi's pay arrangements there aren't known, but he is expected to receive an equity stake in the firm that could be worth tens of millions of dollars following an IPO.

Beyond pay, joining fast-growing technology firms can offer other goodies. Away from the shadows of Wall Street, executives can find a higher profile and increased freedom.

Anthony Noto left Goldman Sachs Group Inc. a year ago and became finance chief at Twitter Inc., the messaging service that the stock market values at $23.7 billion. Ruth Porat traded Morgan Stanley for Google Inc. Vanessa Wittman, formerly of insurer Marsh & McLennan Cos., is at cloud-storage company Dropbox Inc., another business valued in the billions of dollars in private financings.

Ms. Porat was wooed by Google with a compensation package worth around $70 million, including a one-time bonus and new-hire stock grants. That was more than twice what she took home in total during her previous three years at Morgan Stanley, according to securities filings.

The investment appears to be paying quick dividends for the Internet search giant. Strong quarterly earnings released earlier this month jolted its shares, making Google worth $65 billion more overnight. Some analysts partially credited Ms. Porat's May arrival at Google with market-pleasing belt-tightening that was evident in the results.

Google's stock-market value is more than $400 billion, compared with Morgan Stanley's $78 billion.

Technology firms' rise have forced Wall Street to rethink its ways in the name of keeping scarce talent. Some private-equity firms have backed off policies that force their newest hires to obtain M.B.A.s, partly because technology startups and venture-capital firms offer Ivy Leaguers attractive alternatives that often consider expensive business degrees optional.

Several Wall Street firms have adopted rules aimed at reducing the famously long hours spent in the office by their latest recruits to combat burnout and attrition.

Wall Street recruiters and executives say technology companies have boosted competition for workers of all ranks. "There's no border on industries any longer," said Michael Goodman, managing partner of Long Ridge Partners, which recruits employees for investment firms. "Smart is smart."

Still, these moves don't always work out. Greg Smithies decamped for Silicon Valley in 2010, disillusioned with the financial industry after its near collapse. He left Citigroup Inc. to join a venture investing firm. Now, Mr. Smithies is back in New York, working at a three-year-old data analytics firm.

"On the West Coast, you see a lot of news reports of startup founders becoming billionaires before they're 40," the 31-year-old said. "People want to live the dream, but the reality is that you'll end up at a startup and make between $100,000 and $150,000 a year as an engineer, and the startup will go under."

Mr. Tosi's own Silicon Valley gambit isn't without risk. Airbnb's business model, which allows private owners to rent properties for short stays, has met resistance from some lawmakers, and past Internet booms have shown that technology-company valuations can shrivel as dramatically as they balloon.

In part, Mr. Tosi's decision gives him another chance to swing for the fences. He became Blackstone's CFO in 2008, shortly after the firm's stock-market debut, and helped steer its rise to becoming one of Wall Street's marquee firms. Blackstone manages about $333 billion on behalf of pensioners, endowments and the wealthy.

Airbnb began its courtship of Mr. Tosi in the spring, according to people familiar with the matter. It wasn't the first time Mr. Tosi had been approached by a technology company. Apple Inc. made a fruitless attempt to hire him in 2010, according to people familiar with the matter. Mr. Tosi, who in his 30s was Merrill Lynch & Co.'s CFO, chose Airbnb becuase it is a nascent company that he can help shape, while the other technology companies that have tried to lure him were more developed, like Blackstone, according to a person familiar with his decision.

Known inside Blackstone as "L.T.," he has long had a tech bent and an entrepreneurial streak, Blackstone President Hamilton "Tony" James said in an interview. He introduced back-office technology there and helped develop a pair of financial-data companies in which Blackstone invested, and it was known within the firm that he hoped to someday run a technology company.

"Even though he loved Blackstone, that was his aspiration," Mr. James said. "We accepted that, we managed around it and we built around it."

BOOK REVIEW
When ' big data ' was collected by typewriter

Review by Clive Cookson 
721 words
27 July 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
08
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

How Our Days Became Numbered: Risk and the Rise of the Statistical Individual

By Dan Bouk (University of Chicago Press, 28/$40)

The unemployed lumber worker whose 1939 portrait adorns the cover of How Our Days Became Numbered has a "face fit for a film star", as Dan Bouk puts it. But he is not there for his looks. Bouk wants us to focus on his bulging bicep, across which is tattooed "SSN 535-07-5248": his social security number.

The photograph of Thomas Cave by documentary photographer Dorothea Lange illustrates the high water mark of American respect for statistical labelling. Cave was so proud of his newly issued number that he had it inked forever on his arm.

When the Roosevelt administration introduced the federal social security system in the 1930s, it worked out rates of contribution and benefit on the basis of statistical practices already established by life insurance companies. The industry is at the heart of Bouk's history of personal data collection and analysis - because it worked out how to measure and predict the health of ordinary Americans in the late 19th and early 20th centuries.

Life insurers took a financial grip on US middle classes from the 1830s. Their agents convinced male professionals and businessmen of the "need to replace themselves, at least in a monetary sense, upon their deaths, to ensure that their wives . . . never had to work and that their children would not become orphaned wards of the state," Bouk writes.

To qualify for a policy, the applicant had to submit to an investigation by the agent about his age, habits and finances; to agree to set a value on his life; and to undergo a medical examination. In exchange for economic security, applicants allowed themselves to become "commodified risks". The number of Americans in the insurers' corporate files exceeded 10m by 1900.

By the 1930s life insurance was woven so deeply into the nation's economic fabric that, when the Great Depression bit, many families were prepared to maintain their policies at almost any cost. A popular view, quoted by Bouk, was: "We'll give up everything except our life insurance. We can't let that go."

The industry was in better shape to ride out such an economic crisis than when many companies failed in the downturn following the Panic of 1873. Much of Bouk's excellent work deals with the mathematical and actuarial engineering that insurers undertook between the two depressions to fortify their finances and expand their customer base.

At the heart of the exercise was probabilistic risk assessment: predicting the likely longevity of applicants by combining individual medical histories with population-based statistics. Frederick Hoffman of Prudential Insurance toured 100 cemeteries in the southern states with a portable typewriter, gathering birth and death dates of 100,000 people.

The data analysis led to important conclusions that had eluded academic researchers. For instance, in 1908 Brandreth Symonds, medical director of the Mutual Life Insurance Company of New York, warned that contrary to contemporary medical opinion, it was healthier to be thin than fat. Though weight loss was then associated with tuberculosis, overweight people suffered more premature mortality because of their higher rates of heart and kidney disease and diabetes.

Bouk, a historian at New York's Colgate University, brings out the racial tensions intrinsic to the mortality data. Black Americans lived shorter lives, on average, as a result of worse living conditions, and insurers penalised them as a group by offering less favourable terms without regard for individual circumstances. After the civil war, black activists challenged the assumption that past data reliably predicted a group's future mortality. Some states passed laws to stop this sort of discrimination, upon which many companies refused to insure African-Americans at all.

The detailed narrative stops with the second world war, though Bouk includes an epilogue showing how the statistical foundations of risk management, laid down by life insurers and then by government on paper and cards, led later in the 20th century to a " big data " explosion as computers and electronic storage took over. Americans were never again as happy to be numbered and analysed as they had been in 1939.

The writer is the FT's science editor
